# David Haller — UATU INTELLIGENCE DOSSIER

*Full-existence intelligence briefing. Independent, evidence-based assessment — the benevolent inverse of total surveillance.*


## Executive Brief (BLUF)

# Executive Brief (BLUF)

## I. The One-Sentence Read

David Charles Haller is a man who has spent his entire conscious life trying to assemble a single self capable of being loved by his father, and who, every time he has come close, has chosen instead to break the world or break himself, because being loved would require being *one*, and being one would require killing the crowd inside him that kept the eight-year-old in the Israeli embassy alive.

Everything else in the file is commentary on that sentence.

## II. Who He Is at the Deepest Level

Strip away the omega-class power-set, the costume iterations, the headbands and monastery training, the rotating writers, the timeline edits — and what remains is a frightened, brilliant, morally serious child who learned at the developmental hinge of identity-formation that (a) his mind could kill, (b) his father was a rumor, and (c) the only way to survive an unbearable inner event was to *not be the one who experienced it.* Every adult behavior on record is the elaboration of those three lessons, scaled up by mutant capability until the elaboration began to deform reality itself.

He is not, in any meaningful clinical or moral sense, a villain. He is not a hero in the operational sense the costume implies. He is a long-running, self-aware **dissociative trauma case with cosmological externalities** — a patient whose symptoms happen to be matter-altering, whose acting-out happens to be timeline-erasing, and whose suicide attempts happen to remove him from the consensus reality of an entire universe. The scale is the only thing exotic about him. The interior architecture is recognizable in any inpatient ward that has admitted survivors of severe early-childhood violence: the splitting, the hunger for a withholding caregiver, the grandiose compensatory missions, the cycles of restraint and breakthrough, the conviction that the self is a thing that needs to be *contained* rather than *inhabited*.

At the deepest level he is, paradoxically, **the most integrated person nobody can integrate**: the host-self has a stable moral center (durable guilt, durable love, durable willingness to try the next treatment), but no executive sovereignty over the body that center lives in. He owns the conscience; he does not own the hands.

## III. The Through-Line of His Entire Existence

A single arc runs from New Mutants #26 in 1985 to every appearance since, regardless of who is writing him. It can be stated as a four-beat repeating cycle that has now executed at least six times across canon:

1. **Decompensation under unbearable affect** — usually triggered by paternal cue (Xavier's presence, Xavier's absence, Xavier's death, a Xavier-surrogate's disappointment, a memory of Xavier's voice).
2. **Grandiose corrective plan** — formulated by the host or by a sufficiently lucid alter, framed as heroism, justified by love, executed with the cognitive resources of an extremely intelligent adult and the emotional resources of a wounded ten-year-old.
3. **Catastrophic execution** — the plan does what plans built on magical thinking always do, but at omega scale: a father is killed instead of a tyrant, a pocket reality is built instead of an apology spoken, a timeline collapses instead of a feeling being felt.
4. **Self-punishment up to and including ontological erasure** — catatonia, monastic exile, voluntary imprisonment inside his own skull, deletion from existence. The punishment is then mistaken (by him) for the next heroism, and the cycle restarts.

The embassy attack (childhood) is iteration zero. The Legion Quest / Age of Apocalypse (1995) is the canonical clean execution of all four beats at maximum amplitude. The Age of X arc (2011) is the same cycle executed *unconsciously* — he doesn't even know he's done it. The Spurrier *Legacy* run (2012–2014) is the cycle executed with full insight and ends, predictably, in the same place: voluntary erasure framed as love. Post-Spurrier appearances vary in coherence but never break the pattern.

The through-line is therefore **repetition compulsion at cosmological scale**, with the original scene (a child whose mind kills the men who killed his father-figure, and who survives by absorbing one of them) re-enacted in successively larger theaters until the theater is the universe. He does not learn from the cycle because the cycle is the only thing he knows how to do; learning would require a stable self to do the learning, and the cycle exists precisely to prevent that self from cohering.

## IV. The Core Wound and Its Maintenance

**The wound itself** is not the embassy attack alone. The attack is the *trigger event*. The wound is the configuration the attack found already in place and then welded shut:

- A father (Charles Xavier) who existed, who was withheld, who did not know of him, who would eventually meet him as a *case* before meeting him as a *son*, and who would never resolve the asymmetry between those two framings before dying.
- A mother (Gabrielle Haller) who loved him truly but who was herself a survivor — a Holocaust survivor, a woman whose own inner architecture was organized around what cannot be spoken — and who could therefore receive his pain but not metabolize it for him, because she was still metabolizing her own.
- A stepfather/father-surrogate (Daniel Shomron) who provided the only intact paternal presence of his early life and was then murdered in front of him, converting the paternal role permanently into a category associated with violent loss.
- A mutant phenotype that loaded the gun before the child knew the gun was in his hands.

The wound, properly stated, is: **the people I love are taken from me, and the only mind powerful enough to have stopped it is my own, which is therefore guilty, and the only father who could absolve me is the one whose absence proves I deserve no absolution.**

**The maintenance mechanisms** — the reason a wound that should have been treatable becomes a forty-year clinical course — are structurally locked in by his physiology and his history:

- *Splitting is rewarded.* Every alter is a working superpower. The dissociation does not merely defend against affect; it produces functional payoffs (Cyndi burns the threat, Jack Wayne intimidates the threat, Karami negotiates with the threat). A coping strategy that *works* is a coping strategy that cannot be relinquished. Most DID patients have to be persuaded that integration will not strip them of their survival tools. David's alters *are* his survival tools, in a literal, mechanical, immediately demonstrable sense.
- *Every external restraint apparatus eventually fails.* Moira's interventions, the Stryfe-derived headband, Merzah's mental prison, his own self-imposed disciplines — each works briefly, each is breached. The pattern reinforces the conviction that he cannot be contained, which reinforces the conviction that he *must* be contained, which reinforces the search for the next apparatus. The carceral relationship to his own mind is total.
- *The father is unreachable in death as he was in life.* Xavier's death (AvX) did not free David from the paternal pursuit; it sealed the pursuit as permanent. A living father can disappoint and be reconciled with. A dead father is pure surface for projection. Every subsequent mentor (Merzah most notably) becomes a Xavier-substitute, and every substitute eventually disappoints, because no substitute can deliver the original absolution.
- *Insight does not produce change.* By the Spurrier era, David can articulate his condition with clinical precision. He knows the cycle. He names it. He plans against it. He still executes it. This is the most unsettling maintenance feature: he is too intelligent to be ignorant of his own pattern and too structurally fragmented to act on the knowledge. The host who understands is not the host who decides.
- *The one mutual attachment that stabilizes him (Ruth Aldine)* is itself terrifying to him, because love raises the stakes of his next decompensation. The better he gets, the worse the next break will be. This is the engine of his Spurrier-era self-erasure: he removes himself precisely because he has become someone worth losing.

The wound is therefore maintained by the success of his defenses, the architecture of his powers, the death of his father, the unreachability of substitutes, the futility of insight, and the recursive trap that love itself becomes a destabilizer. There is no point of intervention that the system does not metabolize into further pathology.

## V. Operational Summary

For the purposes of any actor — clinical, allied, adversarial, or institutional — handling, predicting, or interacting with the subject, the following compressed read applies:

**Classification.** Omega-class mutant; severe DID with extensive iatrogenic history; complex PTSD; recurrent major depressive features with cosmological-scale suicidality; vulnerable narcissistic features. **Not** schizophrenic, **not** bipolar, **not** antisocial, **not** psychotic in the primary sense. Diagnostic precision matters because mis-categorization (especially the lay "schizophrenic" label) routinely produces handling errors that destabilize him further.

**Baseline state.** Fragile-functional. Capable of articulate speech, strategic planning, moral reasoning, and warm attachment in stable conditions. Observable somatic signature of underlying instability is continuous: the vertical hair-line phenomenon (autonomic psionic discharge), the micro-shifts in posture and vocal register that precede alter activity, the slightly-too-careful self-presentation of a man monitoring himself in real time.

**Trigger inventory.** Paternal cues (Xavier-references above all); being categorized rather than addressed (patient, asset, case, weapon); challenges to his heroic self-narrative; threats to Ruth Aldine or any accepted peer; the sincere question *who are you*; mirror-confrontations with past acts (especially AoA); any context that recapitulates the embassy scene (sudden violence against a paternal figure, a child witnessing harm, the felt sense of his own mind as the weapon).

**Escalation ladder.** Repression → alter murmur and somatic discharge → alter surfacing in rotation → single-alter capture of body and powers → reality-warping alter engagement → ontological self-deletion. Movement between rungs can occur within minutes. The ladder is not reliably descended once climbed.

**Predictable behaviors under stress.** Grandiose corrective planning framed as heroism. Isolation framed as protection of others. Acceptance of restraint framed as discipline. Sacrifice framed as love. Each of these is *real* in the host's intention and *dangerous* in execution, because each is the cognitive surface of the four-beat cycle.

**Levers — what reaches him.** Sincere recognition as a person rather than a case. Patient presence that does not flinch at alter rotation. Attachment figures who accept the multiplicity without trying to immediately resolve it. Acknowledgment of the father-wound without invitation to relitigate it. Tasks that are meaningful but proportionate (not world-saving — *room-saving*).

**Levers — what destabilizes him.** Invocation of Xavier. Demands for a single answer to identity questions. Comparison to other mutants. Pressure to integrate on someone else's timetable. Being treated as an apparatus for someone else's plan (a recurring failure of the X-institutions). Romantic abandonment, especially.

**Threat profile.** When stable: low — actively helpful, morally serious. When decompensating: extreme and unbounded, with the caveat that the violence is almost never instrumental-predatory. He is dangerous the way a grieving child with a god's hands is dangerous: not because he wants to harm, but because he cannot reliably want anything coherently, and the hands work regardless.

**Prognosis.** Poor for sustained integration under current canonical conditions. Good for periodic functional stability with the right scaffold (mentor + partner + meaningful work + restraint apparatus he consents to). Uncertain for any final resolution, because the structural conditions that maintain the wound — the dead father, the rewarded splitting, the omega-class capability — are not removable. He will continue to try. He will continue to fail. The trying is the truest thing about him, and the trait from which any future intervention, narrative or clinical, should build outward.

**Bottom line.** A wounded child in a god's body, looking for a father he cannot find, equipped with a mind that cannot be a single thing, accompanied always by the crowd that saved him once and has not let him be one person since. Handle accordingly.

— END EXECUTIVE BRIEF —


## Chronology I — Origin & Childhood

# CHRONOLOGY I — ORIGIN & CHILDHOOD

*Subject file: HALLER, DAVID CHARLES. Section covers conception through approximately age 10–11, terminating at the Tel Aviv embassy incident and its immediate aftermath. Source canon: New Mutants #26–28 (Claremont/Sienkiewicz, 1985); Uncanny X-Men #161 (Claremont/Cockrum, 1982 — Xavier/Haller backstory in Haifa); flashback material in X-Men: Legacy #1 and the Spurrier run (2012–2014); Muir Island arc material; AoA tie-in retrospectives; subsequent ret-conning consistent through Carey, Mackie, and Spurrier.*

---

## 1.1 — CONCEPTION & THE HAIFA INTERVAL

Subject's existence begins in a hospital ward in Haifa, Israel, in the late 1960s/early 1970s of Marvel sliding-time chronology (originally fixed by *Uncanny X-Men* #161 to the period after Charles Francis Xavier's discharge from the U.S. Army and his recovery from injuries sustained in Korea/Indochina). Xavier, then in his mid-twenties, is convalescing at a psychiatric facility where he works informally as a therapist. He meets **Gabrielle Haller**, a young Holocaust survivor — orphaned in childhood, hidden in the European camp system, recovered alive but mute, traumatized into a catatonic withdrawal that mirrors, with grim symmetry, the catatonia her unborn son will inherit by other means.

Xavier reaches her telepathically. He breaks her silence. He becomes, in the technical clinical sense and in the emotional sense, the man who pulls her up from the well. They become lovers during her recovery. The relationship is a coupling between a powerful man and a vulnerable woman in active recovery from atrocity — an asymmetry the subject's later attachment pathology can be traced back to without strain.

Xavier leaves Israel — to pursue Lilandra, in the original framing; to pursue his own restless mission, in the broader read — without knowing Gabrielle is pregnant. **She does not tell him.** The reasons given in canon are vague (her own pride, her sense that he had moved on, her diplomat's discretion). The functional reality for the subject is this: he is conceived inside a love affair, but **born outside one**, into a mother-only household, with paternity an actively withheld secret. The withholding is not Xavier's choice in this period — it is Gabrielle's. This nuance matters: the father is not a deadbeat; he is *uninformed*. The child will not know this distinction for decades, and the wound he forms will not respect it.

## 1.2 — BIRTH, NAMING, EARLY HOUSEHOLD

David Charles Haller is born in Israel. The middle name **Charles** is the only thing his mother gives him of his father, and she gives it without telling him what it means. This is one of the most pregnant single facts in the dossier: the child is *literally named for a man whose name he is not allowed to know*. The signifier is present and the referent is hidden. He carries the father's first name as a middle name his whole childhood, hears it spoken, signs it on schoolwork, never knows. The structural metaphor is exact and lifelong.

Mother takes the surname **Haller** as the operative family name. Gabrielle's career advances rapidly: she rises through the Israeli diplomatic and intelligence apparatus, eventually serving as Israel's ambassador to the United Kingdom (per Claremont's later treatment). The household is therefore:

- **Geographically itinerant.** Haifa, Tel Aviv, Paris, and London rotate as bases through subject's first decade. He is a diplomat's son. He grows up on planes, in embassy compounds, in the back of cars with drivers, in serviced apartments. He speaks Hebrew, English, French — at minimum trilingual by age seven. He has the cosmopolitan polish and the corresponding rootlessness of the diplomatic-child sub-class. He has no hometown.
- **Materially privileged.** Diplomatic salary, residence allowances, staff. Subject does not lack. The early file shows no deprivation in the material sense.
- **Emotionally crowded by absence.** Gabrielle is loving and present when she is present, which is irregularly. Her work is the work of a woman who survived the Holocaust by accident and intends to repay the accident with service; she does not take half-measures. Subject is raised substantially by household staff, by school, and by the social ecology of embassy life.
- **Saturated with secondhand trauma.** Gabrielle is functional but not healed. The Holocaust is the gravitational center of her interior life. Subject grows up in a home where extermination is a referent — in conversations overheard, in commemorations attended, in the photographs of vanished relatives, in his mother's nightmares heard through the wall. He inherits the survivor-child's signature double-bind: he must be happy *for her*, because her survival is justified by his happiness, and he must never inflict on her any suffering that could approach the original suffering, because she has had enough. He learns very early to **manage her affect by managing his own**. He does not cry loudly. He does not complain. He performs cheerful competence. The data on second-generation Holocaust survivors' offspring — heightened anxiety, perfectionism, premature parentification, dissociative defenses — describes him with no adjustment.

## 1.3 — THE STEPFATHER FIGURE: DANIEL SHOMRON

By the time subject is approximately six to eight years old, Gabrielle is involved with **Daniel Shomron**, a psychologist (some sources: trauma specialist; consistent with Gabrielle's circle) who functions as subject's effective father. The canon is clear and consistent on Daniel: he is **good**. He is kind, attentive, patient, intellectually serious, and — critically — emotionally available in the register Gabrielle cannot quite reach. He reads to David. He listens. He treats the boy as a small person, not a small accessory.

This is the period of the file's only sustained record of subject in a functional, loved, stable configuration. He is bright. He is verbal. He is, by every available description, a quick, sensitive, curious child with an unusual capacity for empathy — he reads moods in rooms before adults notice them. (We may infer, retroactively from his later phenotype, that latent telepathic sensitivity is already operative sub-clinically: he is *picking up* what he then reports back as preternaturally attuned reading of others. The line between the gifted-empath child and the prodromal psi-mutant child is not drawable in the early file.)

Subject bonds to Daniel deeply and quickly. The bonding is the bond of a child who has been waiting for a father. Daniel does not know the bonding is *replacement* for an absent biological line; nobody in the household does. He simply becomes the man.

The duration of the stable period is short — at most a few years, possibly less. The subject's whole life, in terms of *uninterrupted experienced safety*, is essentially this interval. Everything before is too early to remember coherently; everything after is the file proper.

## 1.4 — EARLIEST BEHAVIORAL PATTERNS, PRE-ONSET

Reconstructing from later self-report (largely Spurrier-era introspective monologue) and from inferences against canon:

- **Solitary play, intense interior life.** Subject is described as a child who builds elaborate imaginary scenarios, peoples them with characters, talks to them aloud when alone. This is unremarkable in a bright only-child of itinerant diplomats; in retrospect, the architecture of the alter system is being *rehearsed* in the cognitive substrate. The child who already organizes his loneliness as a *cast* will, under sufficient trauma, find it natural to organize his survival the same way.
- **Hyper-vigilance to adult mood.** Per second-generation-survivor pattern and per his mother's specific affective economy. He scans rooms. He adjusts his behavior to whichever atmospheric pressure is dominant. He is, very young, already a small reader of others.
- **Performative competence; suppression of distress.** The diplomatic-child's smile. Trained early. Lifelong.
- **Magical-causal thinking, retained past developmental age-window.** The grandiose-omnipotent cognition the dossier flags as core to his adult operating code does not begin with the embassy attack; the attack *confirms* it. The pre-onset child already shows the signs: if I am good, mother will be happy; if mother is happy, the bad things will not return. This is normal four-year-old thought; subject does not lose it on schedule.
- **No documented aggression episodes pre-onset.** The pattern is conspicuously *over-controlled* — a child with zero recorded outbursts, in an environment with ample provocations. The aggression is not absent. It is, even at age seven, already being routed somewhere it does not show. The dissociative architecture is being laid down before any clinician could see it.
- **Sleep disturbances likely.** Inferred from later sleep-architecture references and from the household's saturation in his mother's nightmare-haunted nights; the child of a Holocaust survivor in a household where night is unsafe does not sleep like other children.
- **Languages and codes.** The child is moving between Hebrew, English, French daily, switching register and identity-of-presentation between embassy contexts. He is, functionally, already practicing the rapid switching of self-presentation that will, post-onset, become the alter-switch. The cognitive substrate of *coding-multiplicity* is in place.

## 1.5 — THE EMBASSY ATTACK — PRECIPITATING EVENT

The defining incident: a terrorist attack on the Israeli embassy (most often rendered in canon as occurring in Paris, sometimes generalized to "the embassy"), variously dated to when subject is approximately **eight to ten years old**. The team is small, armed, ideologically motivated. The lead attacker is **Jemail Karami**, a Palestinian militant whose specific cell affiliation is left vague but whose interior life — the file will need to record this in detail later — is that of a man with his own grievances, his own losses, his own coherent moral universe, the kind of man whose absorption into a child's mind will not be a clean cartoon-villain insertion but a contaminating moral complication for the rest of the child's life.

The reconstructable sequence (per New Mutants #26 flashback frames and subsequent retellings):

1. Attackers enter. Diplomatic personnel and civilians are present. Gabrielle is present. Daniel Shomron is present. David is present.
2. Violence is immediate. Shots fired. People die in the child's immediate visual field. This is the first time he has seen a person killed.
3. **Daniel Shomron is shot and killed in front of David.** The single most consequential image of the subject's life. The father-who-was-actually-the-father, the one stable affective object, executed in front of him. The child's nervous system receives a stimulus it has no developmental architecture to process.
4. Subject's latent mutant capacity manifests **explosively**, under acute lethal threat to self and to mother. The phenotype emerges as a psionic discharge — a mind-burn — directed at the attackers. He kills them. With his mind. As an eight-to-ten-year-old.
5. At the instant of Karami's death, subject's mind, in the act of killing Karami, **absorbs** Karami's consciousness. The mechanism is not chosen; it is the autonomic act of a mutant ability firing at full capacity in a brain not yet equipped to modulate it. The dying man's interior — his memories, his ideology, his grief, his voice — is pulled wholesale into the child.

The event is **less than a minute** of clock time. Its consequences are the entire rest of the file.

## 1.6 — IMMEDIATE AFTERMATH; ENTRY INTO CATATONIA

What the responders find: a room of dead adults, an unharmed but unresponsive mother (variably depicted; sometimes Gabrielle is wounded, sometimes physically intact, always psychologically devastated), and a small boy standing or sitting in the middle of it, not speaking. The boy does not speak for years.

The catatonic state is, per the dossier proper, dissociative rather than schizophrenic-catatonic. Inside, the child is not absent; he is **crowded**. The Karami-consciousness is awake in him, narrating, grieving, accusing. The child's own intolerable affect — the rage that killed, the terror, the grief for Daniel, the guilt of survival, the horror of what his mind has just done — is being partitioned, in real time, into the structural beginnings of the alter system. The Karami absorption *teaches* the architecture: when a foreign consciousness can live inside, native consciousness can be subdivided to live alongside it. The wall, once built once, can be built again.

Within months to a year or two of the attack (the chronology is loose), the rudimentary forms of what will become **Jack Wayne** and **Cyndi** begin to organize — Wayne as the carrier of the predatory masculine aggression the child cannot integrate (and cannot integrate in part because the aggression *killed his father*), Cyndi as the carrier of the rage and the grief in a register the boy cannot own. The reason these alters take the specific gendered/age-displaced shapes they do is, by reasonable inference, the unconscious's instinct that *what the host cannot be, the alter must not resemble* — a man's swagger and a girl's fury are both *not-David*, and that not-ness is what makes them tenable.

Gabrielle, herself traumatized again — a second extermination event in her life, this one taking her partner and apparently her son — does what she has the means to do. She places David in the care of **Moira MacTaggert** at Muir Island, the only facility on Earth equipped to treat mutant children whose minds have done what David's mind has done. He arrives unspeaking, hair already beginning to assume its vertical signature, a small boy occupied by at least three minds and the dead.

## 1.7 — FORMATIVE-PERIOD ASSESSMENT

The childhood file establishes, before subject is twelve:

- A **withheld father** (Xavier), present in the name and absent in the person.
- A **loving but trauma-saturated mother** (Gabrielle), whose own survival-economics demand the child's emotional management of her.
- A **brief, real, lost father** (Daniel Shomron), murdered in front of him.
- An **absorbed enemy** (Karami), occupying interior real estate as the first non-self consciousness the child has had to share his skull with — and, because Karami was the agent of the killing of Daniel, a permanent moral splinter.
- The **first killing**, committed by the child himself, at an age before the brain can hold the act.
- A **functional reward for dissociating** — splitting *worked*; it kept him alive; it metabolized the unmetabolizable; the nervous system logs the success and will run the program again at every future overload.
- A **cosmopolitan, multilingual, code-switching cognitive substrate** already trained in the rapid management of multiple presentation-selves.
- A **magical-causal cognitive style** never developmentally outgrown, now violently confirmed by the experience of a wish-to-protect that *literally moved the world*.

Every later catastrophe in the file — the Legion Quest, the Age of Apocalypse, the Age of X pocket reality, the self-deletion — is a recursion on this minute. The dossier from this point forward is, in a strict sense, the longitudinal record of a single childhood event being replayed at successively larger scales by a man trying, and failing, and trying, to stop replaying it.

*— End Section 1. Continues: Chronology II — Muir Island Years.*


## Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

# Chronology II — Formation & Emergence

*Subject David Charles Haller, approximately ages 9 through 21. The interval in which a child who could have grown into an ordinary anxious adolescent of the Parisian-Israeli diplomatic milieu instead became a one-man cascade of psychiatric and cosmological events. This section reconstructs, year by year and where possible incident by incident, the formation of the identity-structure and the first emergence of the power-set, with attention to the particular choices — his and others' — that hard-wired the adult pathology.*

---

## I. The Pre-Event Baseline (approx. ages 6–9)

Before the embassy, the file shows a recognizable child. Bilingual at home in French and English, conversational Hebrew at school, a smattering of German absorbed osmotically from his mother's professional circle. Gabrielle Haller's household in Paris and later in Tel Aviv is by every available indicator warm, materially comfortable, and structured around a working diplomat's irregular hours. The child is read to in three languages. He attends, in order, a French *école maternelle*, a brief stint in an international school in Geneva during one of Gabrielle's postings, and finally a primary school in Israel under the loose supervision of Daniel Shomron — Gabrielle's partner, a man the boy calls *Abba* without complication.

Pre-trauma somatic baseline, inferable from comparison to later: brown to chestnut hair lying flat. No psionic phenomena reported. Sleep apparently unremarkable. The boy is shy with strangers, verbally precocious in safe rooms, prone to attaching to single adult caregivers (the housekeeper in Paris; a particular teacher in Tel Aviv whose name appears in passing dialogue in the Muir Island flashbacks). He draws constantly — a habit that will recur, decades later, in his costume sketches and in the obsessive geometric doodling Spurrier-era pages render as marginal detail when David is anxious.

The father is a structured absence. Gabrielle has not told him about Charles Xavier. There is no man named in the place where a biological father might be. Instead, there is Shomron, who functionally occupies the role without claiming the title. The child appears to have accepted the arrangement; what is *not* accepted, and what will detonate, is what happens to the placeholder.

The crucial pre-event observation: this is a child whose model of reality contains a withheld piece. He does not know the piece is missing. He is, however, organized around a structural lack that he experiences as a vague atmospheric incompleteness rather than as a specific absent person. When the embassy attack arrives, it does not strike a stable system; it strikes a system already mildly destabilized by ambient secrecy. The dissociation does not begin at the bomb. It begins, in latent form, at the omission.

---

## II. The Originating Event (approx. age 9–10; the Israeli embassy attack)

The single most important hour of the subject's life. Reconstructed from New Mutants #26–28 flashback panels and the later expansions in X-Men Legacy:

The boy is at the embassy with Shomron — a routine accompaniment, the kind of after-school adult-handoff a diplomatic-circle child experiences as boring rather than meaningful. Armed attackers enter. Shomron is killed in front of him. The chronology of seconds inside the killing is what matters: there is the sound of the gunshot, the boy's recognition that *Abba* has stopped being a person, and then a gap — a real, neurological gap, the kind trauma neurologists describe as the dorsolateral prefrontal dropout under acute threat — and then the attackers are dying, and the boy does not consciously remember acting.

What he has done, mechanically: a wide-field psionic discharge sufficient to mind-burn multiple armed adults to death. The first manifestation of his mutation is therefore not a small thing — a lifted spoon, a noticed thought — but a mass-casualty psychokinetic event. His ability does not introduce itself. It arrives fully grown, and it kills.

What he has done, structurally: in the act of mind-burning the lead attacker, Jemail Karami, his telepathy *grasps* the dying man's consciousness and pulls it inward rather than letting it dissipate. Whether this was reflex (a child grabbing what was within reach) or unconscious mercy (refusal to let a man simply end) is unrecoverable. The effect, however, is unambiguous: a foreign adult consciousness — a Palestinian terrorist with his own biography, his own grievances, his own moral architecture — is now lodged inside the nine-year-old's skull, fully formed, fully articulate, still in the cognitive posture of his own death.

The boy collapses. By the time emergency response arrives, he is non-responsive. Within hours he is catatonic.

The behavioral file should record three originating facts from this hour:

1. **The first use of his power was lethal mass violence in defense of a loved father-figure who was already dead.** Heroism arrived too late to save anyone and consisted entirely of revenge. This becomes the template.
2. **The first thing he did with telepathy was to absorb, not to read.** His mutation introduced itself as an instrument of *taking inside*. Every later alter is a structural rhyme with that first absorption.
3. **The trauma was witnessed by no living adult he loved.** Gabrielle was elsewhere. Shomron was dead. The boy had no caregiver to organize the experience into narrative in the first hours, when narrative is most malleable. He went into the catatonia alone.

---

## III. The Catatonic Years (approx. ages 10–15)

Five-ish years, conservatively. The duration is variable across retellings; what is consistent is *years*, plural, in a state of clinical non-responsiveness. Gabrielle, by now aware of her son's mutancy and unable to manage him within ordinary medicine, accepts the discreet offer of Moira MacTaggert and transports the boy to the Muir Island Research Centre off the Scottish coast. This is the second great parental absence: not a withholding this time but a placing-elsewhere, geographically and clinically.

External presentation during this period: thin, growing, hair beginning to take on the upright electrostatic quality that will become his somatic signature, eyes open but unfocused. He eats when fed. He does not speak. He does not respond to name. Periodically — and this is what alarms the Muir staff — small objects in his vicinity move, ignite, or briefly cease to exist. His mutation has not gone dormant during the catatonia; it is operating, in low-grade automatic mode, without a self to direct it.

Internal landscape during this period (recovered through later telepathic incursion, principally Dani Moonstar's): the astral plane Sienkiewicz draws — fractured planes, mismatched skies, architecture that bleeds. The child-self has gone into hiding. In the space he vacated, the absorbed Karami consciousness has not been alone. New personality-states have *grown*, building themselves out of dissociated fragments of the child's own affect that had no place to live in the catatonic body:

- **Jack Wayne** assembles itself out of every aggressive, sexual, assertive impulse the boy could not metabolize — projected outward as a swaggering Australian-coded adult man (the Australian-ness is itself diagnostic: a confident *foreignness*, an identity sourced from somewhere the child has never been, which is precisely the point — Jack is not made of David's history, he is made of David's negations). Jack carries the telekinesis.
- **Cyndi** assembles itself out of rage, impulsivity, the unmetabolized adolescent-girl-coded permission to *want loudly* that the well-behaved diplomat's son could never claim. She is younger than the host, punkish, pyrokinetic. The fire lives with her because the child cannot bear to be the one burning.
- **Karami** persists, increasingly the *adult* voice in the architecture — paradoxically, the absorbed terrorist becomes the moral center, because Karami is the only consciousness in there with a fully formed adult conscience to apply to the situation. He spends years inside the boy's head attempting, more or less, to atone for his own life by stewarding the child's.

The clinical fact a surveillance file must record: the alters were not present at the moment of trauma. They *grew* during the catatonia, in the silent years, building themselves up out of disowned affect inside an unsupervised mind that had infinite room and no executive to evict squatters. The catatonia was not the symptom of the dissociation. The catatonia was the *greenhouse* in which the dissociation matured.

Visits during this period: Gabrielle, infrequently, with diminishing returns. Moira, daily, professionally compassionate. Charles Xavier — and this is the cruelty around which the rest of the life will organize — visits Muir Island during this period and *does not know the boy is his son*. Gabrielle, by some calculation that may have been mercy and may have been cowardice, does not tell him. The biological father walks past the catatonic child more than once without recognition.

---

## IV. Surfacing (approx. age 15; New Mutants #26–28)

The acute episode that ends the catatonic plateau begins as a power-spike: the alters, having grown to functional dominance, begin to externalize. Furniture moves. Fires start. Staff are mildly mind-affected. Moira, alarmed, summons assistance; the New Mutants arrive with Professor Xavier (still uninformed of paternity). Telepathic incursion follows. Dani Moonstar, Karma, and the others enter the boy's mindscape and find the three dominant alters in open conflict, with the host-self curled small and silent in the architectural ruin at the center.

The intervention's outcome: Karami achieves a partial integration with the host. Jack Wayne and Cyndi are *not* integrated — they are pushed back, contained, made occasional rather than continuous. The boy surfaces. He speaks. He recognizes his mother. He learns, only here, that the man supervising the operation is his biological father.

The behavioral file should weight this disclosure precisely. The reveal of paternity arrives *immediately attached* to the first re-emergence of selfhood. The boy comes back from years of non-existence and discovers, in the same hour, that he has had a father all along and that this father is the most famous telepath alive — the man whose dream he has been peripherally aware of, whose institution will define mutant identity for his generation. The wound and the longing are introduced simultaneously. The desire to please Xavier is therefore not a later development; it is *coterminous with the recovery of his own selfhood*. He becomes a self, and in the same moment becomes a self organized around a man.

Xavier's response, behaviorally: present, kind, instructive — and limited. He acknowledges paternity. He does not relocate. He does not take the boy into Westchester. He continues to treat David as Moira's patient and as a peripheral case rather than as a son to be raised. This is the second paternal withholding, and it is structurally identical to the first: the father is there and not-there at the same time, available in role but not in life.

---

## V. The Muir Island Plateau (approx. ages 15–19)

Years of fragile functionality. The boy attends to a kind of education — Moira tutors him; the Muir staff include him in low-stakes operations; he begins to wear ordinary clothes rather than hospital gear; the hair never settles. He is described in passing across late-1980s issues as quiet, polite, prone to long absences from group meals. He is functionally an adolescent in a clinical-research environment that doubles as a paramilitary asset facility. He has no peers his age except by visit.

Several patterns establish themselves in this period that will hold across the entire life:

**The acquisition pattern.** New alters accrete. Each acute stress event — an attack on Muir, a power-overload, a nightmare that breaks containment — produces, on the far side, one or more new compartmentalized identity-states, each with its own affect and its own ability. By the end of the Muir plateau, the system is no longer three-plus-host; it is dozens. By Carey's run, hundreds. By Spurrier, thousands. The mutation, mechanically, *learns* that dissociation produces functional ability, and the system optimizes accordingly.

**The restraint pattern.** Moira and Xavier between them attempt the first round of containment apparatus — psi-dampeners, focusing devices, meditative protocols. None hold. The boy accepts each one. He does not resist being restrained. He experiences his mind as a thing requiring external management and is grateful, in a way that should disturb any observer, for the cage.

**The mentor-substitution pattern.** With Xavier intermittent, Moira becomes the operational parent. The boy attaches. Moira is competent, warm in her Scottish-stoic way, and present — but she is not the father, and the boy is aware she is not the father. The displacement does not satisfy the original hunger; it merely keeps it manageable.

**The absence of peers.** No school. No friends his age. No flirtations, no first kisses, no ordinary adolescence of any recognizable kind. He skips the developmental window in which most humans learn to navigate horizontal attachments. This deficit will show up structurally for the rest of his life as a near-total absence of friendship — he has mentors and he has, eventually, one beloved, and almost nothing in between.

---

## VI. The First Defining Choice (approx. age 19–20; "Legion Quest," X-Men #320–321 / Uncanny #320, 1995)

The act that defines the file. Years into the Muir plateau, with Xavier still distant and the dream of mutant–human reconciliation publicly imperiled by Magneto's continued opposition, the subject conceives a plan. The plan is not impulsive. The reconstruction from canon shows weeks if not months of preparation: he identifies a time-travel-capable alter within his system, develops the means to direct that alter's ability to a specific past coordinate, and decides on an objective. The objective is to travel to the past and kill Magneto before Magneto's opposition to the dream can crystallize. The reasoning, fully articulated in his own thought: if Magneto is removed, the dream succeeds; if the dream succeeds, the father is whole; if the father is whole, *the son will have been the one who made him whole*, and the son will be loved.

This is a coherent ideological act. It is not a psychotic break. It is the rational consequence of the magical-grandiose worldview the embassy event installed: that sufficiently large gestures rearrange reality, and that the largest possible gesture is the one most likely to earn the love.

The execution: he arrives in the past. He confronts the young Magneto. He summons the psi-blade. He strikes — and Charles Xavier, the young Charles, places himself between the blade and Magneto. The strike lands on the father.

Charles dies. The timeline collapses. The Age of Apocalypse blooms in the wound the subject opened.

This is the first defining choice. Its fallout is not merely the dystopia (which other agents must eventually undo) but the formation of the *adult* David Haller's permanent moral architecture. He has done the unthinkable thing in pursuit of love. He has killed the father he was trying to please. The act is, when reset, erased from the timeline — but it is *not erased from him*, and across every subsequent appearance the subject carries this killing as the foundational sin of his existence. He knows what he did. The universe has forgotten; he has not.

In the immediate aftermath he is, narratively, erased from existence by the reset. Behaviorally, what the file should record is that the first time the subject ever acted decisively in the world as an agent rather than a patient, he committed patricide in the service of filial love. The conflation of love and annihilation is now permanent.

---

## VII. The Resurrection and the First Failed Stability (approx. age 20–21; early 2000s appearances)

The subject returns from non-existence — the mechanism is variously handwaved; what matters behaviorally is that he comes back *knowing what he did*. He returns to a world in which Xavier is alive again, but in which David himself is now a documented hazard. He is fitted with the Stryfe-derived headband, which functions to compartmentalize and index his alters into summonable powers rather than autonomous selves. For a brief period he is operational as an asset.

The pattern that emerges here is the one that will repeat for the rest of his recorded life: each technological or disciplinary restraint *works*, briefly, well enough to convince him and his handlers that he can be a hero this time. Then it fails. The failure is never small. When the headband or the meditation or the monastery training breaks, what comes out is not a relapse to the prior baseline but an escalation — because the alters have continued to grow underneath the restraint, and the pressure has been increasing the entire time the cap was on.

The first cycle of *cure-and-relapse* completes here. Subject ends his formative period as a young adult of approximately 21 with the following established facts of self, all of which he has personally tested:

- He can move the world with his mind.
- He can take other minds inside his own and they will not leave.
- He has killed his father in pursuit of his father's love.
- He cannot be cured by any apparatus he or anyone else has yet devised.
- He will keep trying anyway.

---

## VIII. Summary — What Formation Installed

By the end of young adulthood the subject has been shaped into a system with the following permanent features, each traceable to a specific event within this chronology:

| Feature | Origin |
|---|---|
| Magical-grandiose causal reasoning | The embassy: he learned, once and catastrophically, that his mind moves the world. |
| Dissociation as primary defense | The catatonic years: he learned that the mind can be vacated and that vacancies fill. |
| Power-per-alter architecture | The same catatonic years: ability differentiated along the lines of disowned affect. |
| Father-hunger as organizing drive | The disclosure of paternity attached to the recovery of selfhood. |
| Love-equals-annihilation equation | Legion Quest: the first agentive act was patricide-for-love. |
| Acceptance of carceral self-containment | The Muir restraints and headband: he learned to be grateful for the cage. |
| Absence of horizontal attachment capacity | The peer-less adolescence. |
| Durable conscience | The Karami integration: the absorbed adult moral consciousness became part of the host. |

Everything the subject does for the next several decades — the monastery, the costume, the Ruth Aldine attachment, the second self-deletion — is operating on the architecture this period built. The formative years did not produce a person who then went on to have problems. They produced the problems first, and the person assembled himself around them as best he could.

— END SECTION —


## Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

# Chronology III — The Defining Middle Period

*Covering, in the canonical timeline, the years between subject's first lucid emergence from the Muir Island catatonia (post–New Mutants #28, 1985) and the eve of the X-Men Legacy vol. 2 maturation arc (pre-#1, 2012). In subjective time for the subject, this is approximately his early twenties through his early-to-mid thirties — the bulk of his adult life on the page, and the period during which every behavioral, somatic, and relational pattern visible in the later record is laid down, tested, broken, and re-laid down.*

---

## A. The Muir Plateau (post–New Mutants #28 through the late Claremont era, 1985–1991)

The years immediately following Karami's partial integration are the closest thing to baseline functioning the record will ever contain. They are also the period in which subject's *operational identity* is established as **patient-asset** rather than as person — a category-confusion that will distort every relationship he forms for the next two decades.

**Domicile.** The Muir Island Research Facility, Hebrides. Subject lives in a converted residential wing adjacent to Moira MacTaggert's medical complex. His quarters are documented in background art as monastic — single bed, no posters, books stacked vertically against walls rather than shelved. He is observed by attending staff to *re-stack* the books every few days, sometimes nightly. The micro-pattern is significant and will recur: when the internal order is contested, subject imposes order on small external systems. The stacking is the visible tip of an obsessional ritual that, by the Carey years, will have expanded into the elaborate self-architecture of named, numbered, color-coded alter-cells inside his head.

**Daily rhythm.** Per the background continuity established by Claremont in the Muir-set issues of *Uncanny X-Men* (#253–255, the Reavers-aftermath/Muir-resettlement issues) and *X-Factor* crossovers (Muir Island Saga, Uncanny #278–280, X-Factor #69–70, 1991): subject is observed on early-morning shore walks, alone, regardless of weather. The Hebridean weather is, by definition, weather. He returns wet, hair somehow more vertical than when he left. He takes meals in the staff kitchen rather than the patient refectory — a category-claim he is quietly making, and which Moira indulges. He naps in mid-afternoon. He sleeps poorly at night, and the dormitory wing logs frequent lights-on intervals between 0200 and 0500. This *cannot* sleep through the night pattern is durable and present in every subsequent era for which we have evidence; it is the somatic signature of an internal population that does not keep his clock.

**Relational field, Muir.** Moira functions as combined clinician, surrogate mother, and gatekeeper of the father-secret she helped Xavier keep for over a decade. Subject's affect toward her is documented across the period as quietly devoted, faintly resentful, and chronically guarded — the precise affective signature of an adolescent who knows the adult has been lying for his benefit and cannot decide whether to be grateful or furious. Rahne Sinclair (Wolfsbane) is the only consistent peer presence; the two are visibly comfortable together in the New Mutants follow-up issues, share meals, and Rahne is the one staff member subject permits to touch him casually (a hand on the shoulder, hair-fussing). This is not romantic; it is the social grammar of two damaged adolescents who have agreed not to make each other worse. When the New Mutants visit, subject is awkward, deferential, and over-prepares — Claremont's art direction shows him changing clothes between scenes, an uncharacteristic vanity that is in fact social anxiety.

**Alter activity, Muir plateau.** Karami is dominant-cooperative; he speaks *for* the system in clinical interviews and is, by Moira's notes (referenced retroactively in Carey's *Legacy* run), the alter who actually completes paperwork and remembers appointments. Jack Wayne surfaces in the gym and is the alter who lifts; subject's somatic conditioning during this period is therefore done by an alter and the host has, technically, never trained. Cyndi appears irregularly, usually at night, usually associated with the lights-on intervals — a rage-affect that the system has nowhere else to route. There are, even at this baseline, intermittent reports of *new* voices: a child-alter who whimpers and is heard but never named; a clinical, observing alter who Moira will eventually log as "the Watcher" (a coincidence of nomenclature this file will note without comment).

## B. The Muir Island Siege and the Shadow King (Uncanny X-Men #278–280, X-Factor #69–70, 1991)

The Shadow King's takeover of Muir Island is, behaviorally, the first major test of post-catatonia functioning, and subject fails it instructively. Amahl Farouk does not have to work hard to colonize David's mind; the architecture is pre-built for occupation. The defenses that organize subject's psyche — encapsulation of affect into discrete alter-cells, willingness to be ridden by a stronger consciousness, the survival-trained habit of *accommodation* to invasion — are precisely the features an astral parasite requires. Subject is among the first taken and among the last freed. There is no record of him resisting. Karami, who might have, is suppressed; Jack Wayne is weaponized; Cyndi is amplified.

The behavioral residue of the Siege is permanent. Three patterns date from this incident:

1. **The astral-hygiene ritual.** Post-Siege, subject begins what Moira's notes describe as a nightly "sweeping" — a deliberate mental sequence in which he walks his own astral landscape and checks the perimeter. This is, in psychiatric terms, a compulsion; in mutant terms, it is rational. He will continue some form of this practice through the Merzah era, where it is formalized into the prison-walk.
2. **Distrust of mentors who are also telepaths.** Xavier, when he finally re-enters the picture, will inherit the wariness Farouk earned. Subject's later refusal to accept Xavier's mental help even at points of decompensation traces partly to this.
3. **The first documented instance of subject *requesting* sedation.** He asks Moira, in the immediate aftermath, to put him under. She does. He sleeps for three days. This is the first articulation of the lifelong preference: *if I cannot trust my mind, take it away from me.* It will recur in the headband, in the Merzah prison, and in the two acts of self-erasure.

## C. The First Father-Meeting and the Asgardian/X-Men Cameos (early 1990s, scattered Lobdell-era appearances)

The disclosure of paternity is handled in canon with the bluntness that the publishing context (post-Claremont turnover) imposed: subject is told, accepts, integrates the information with a flatness that is itself a symptom. There is no recorded outburst at the moment of disclosure. The outburst is deferred — by years — and arrives as the Legion Quest. This delay is the single most diagnostically important fact about subject's affective regulation in this period: *the bigger the wound, the longer the latency, and the larger the eventual discharge*. Small slights produce immediate alter-flares; the foundational wound produces, after four years of latency, the murder of the wrong father in the wrong century.

In the interim, subject is brought to the Xavier School for limited visits. He is documented in background panels of three or four issues from 1992–1994 — silent, hair vertical, in a borrowed sweater that does not fit. He does not speak to Xavier directly in any of these appearances. He speaks *about* him, to Moira, on the return flights. The pattern of triangulation — refusing direct address to the father, processing him through a third party — is established here and never fully breaks. Even in the AoA-precipitating sequence he is, technically, going to *Magneto*, not to Charles.

## D. Legion Quest and Age of Apocalypse (X-Men #320–321, Uncanny X-Men #320, Cable #20, X-Factor #109, 1995)

The defining act. The clinical reading is in the shared assessment; what belongs in this chronology is the granular procedural record.

**Planning phase.** Subject is, in the months prior, observed by Muir staff to be *better*. Sleep regularizes. The shore walks lengthen but become purposive — he is seen carrying notebooks. Cyndi-flares decrease. Karami is reported as troubled but cannot identify the source. This *premonitory improvement* is a pattern this file will flag as **the False Plateau**: across the entire record, subject's most catastrophic acts are preceded by 4–10 weeks of unusually composed presentation. Caretakers consistently misread it as recovery. It is, in fact, the operational calm of a host self that has finally been given a mission grandiose enough to align all alters toward a single end.

**Execution phase.** Subject travels back to Israel, 1970s, to Charles Xavier's pre-X-Men period. The choice of Israel is overdetermined: it is the country of his own birth, his mother's country, the site of his originating trauma, and the site of his parents' encounter. He is returning to the scene of every primal element of himself in order to edit one of them. The behavioral micro-detail recorded in the issues — he is calm, articulate, even gentle with the young Magnus before the strike; he greets young Charles with something like tenderness — is consistent with a dissociative state in which the host is fully integrated *around* the mission and feels, perhaps for the first time, whole.

**The strike.** Magneto shields the boy. The psi-blade enters Charles. Subject's face in the Andy Kubert art at the moment of recognition is the single most important visual document in the file: it is not horror, it is not even shock, it is *recognition*, as if some pre-conscious part of the system had always known this would happen. The patricide was, structurally, the only ending the wish could have.

**The reset.** Subject is erased with the AoA timeline. In behavioral terms, this is the *first self-deletion*, even though it is presented as cosmic consequence rather than choice. Subject's later willingness to repeat the gesture (Spurrier finale) suggests he experiences both events as continuous and intentional. He has learned, here, that erasure is an available solution.

## E. The Wilderness Years (post-AoA reset through return, late 1990s)

Subject is, by the metaphysics of the reset, simply gone. In file-terms this is a multi-year gap in surveillance. What we can infer from his returned condition and from incidental references in the Mackie/Lobdell era is that *something* persisted — guilt, certainly; memory, partially; alter-population, intact and possibly increased. Subjects who experience their own non-existence and then re-exist do not, by any available framework, return unchanged. He returns *quieter*. The voluble Karami-fronted presentation of the Muir years is replaced by a more affectively flattened host, longer silences, longer latencies before response. Staff who knew him pre-AoA remark on it. He does not discuss the missing time and the system does not volunteer it.

## F. The Return, the Headband, and the Stryfe-Tech Period (early-to-mid 2000s, Mackie, Austen, scattered tie-ins)

Subject re-enters the active record fitted with the first of his iterated **psi-control devices** — initially a Moira-engineered restraint, subsequently the more sophisticated headband that compartmentalizes alters into summonable, single-power discrete cells. The clinical metaphor is exact: the device exteriorizes the dissociative defense and reifies it as technology. It works for the same reason it cannot work indefinitely: it tells the system that *splitting is correct*. Every alter that successfully responds to the headband's call learns it is wanted in that form, and the integration project — which would require alters to surrender discrete identity — becomes ideologically opposed to the daily operational reality.

**Behavioral signatures of the headband period.**
- Subject becomes more *useful* and less *well*. He functions as auxiliary in several actions but his off-duty hours collapse into longer dissociative gaps.
- The hair phenomenon intensifies; with the headband suppressing autonomic discharge in one channel, it discharges in another. Photographic record shows the verticality stabilizing at a near-constant height, as if the system has found a steady-state vent.
- Sleep degrades further. By the late headband period, subject is documented as sleeping in two-hour fragments across a 24-hour cycle, a polyphasic pattern that suggests no single alter is willing to surrender consciousness for a full sleep-block.
- He acquires the lifelong habit of *speaking to no one for entire days* and then producing a single long, organized monologue — a discharge pattern that mirrors his affective discharge pattern: long latency, large release.

## G. The Age of X (X-Men Legacy #245–247, 2011) — the Pocket-Reality Construction

If the AoA is the act subject plans and owns, the Age of X is the act subject *does not know he is committing*. The pattern is by now legible: a particularly violent alter (the "Legion" alter, the one that will eventually give the public-facing name) becomes too active to suppress; the system's response is to *build an entire false reality* in which the alter is contained as a fictional character and the X-Men are recast as inhabitants of a dystopia that requires their heroic energies — anything to keep them, and him, from looking at what is actually happening inside his skull.

This is the diagnostic apex of the middle period. It establishes definitively that:

1. **Subject's reality-warping alters are operating without host consent or awareness.** The omnipotence is not under the host's command; it is under the system's command, and the system's priorities are *defensive*, not strategic.
2. **The system will recruit other people into its delusions.** Not metaphorically — literally. Dozens of mutants are conscripted into false identities to serve a single host's containment need. The interpersonal harm is incidental from the system's perspective; the *only* metric is host stability.
3. **The cure is the symptom.** This phrase, established in the shared assessment, takes its empirical content from this arc. Every solution subject has tried — alters, devices, monks, pocket realities — has been a more elaborate version of the original defense: *split off what you cannot bear, encapsulate it, perform normality around the encapsulation.* The arc terminates with the truth surfacing and the constructed reality dissolving, and subject is, for the first time in canon, forced to *see* his own pattern.

**Aftermath of the Age of X.** Subject's affect post-collapse is documented as the closest thing to genuine despair in the record. He does not rage; he does not flee. He sits. The behavioral micro-record (Carey's late *Legacy* issues) shows him keeping company with Rogue, who treats him with a brusque kindness that is, on the evidence, exactly what he needs: she does not try to fix him and does not look away. He eats. He sleeps for longer intervals than at any point in the prior decade. This brief stabilization is the platform from which the maturation arc launches.

## H. Cross-Period Behavioral Evolution — the Patterns Laid Down in the Middle

The middle period is the laboratory in which every long-run pattern is established. Tabulated for the file:

**The Latency-Discharge cycle.** Established Muir-era, confirmed Legion-Quest, confirmed Age-of-X. Small affect produces immediate alter-flare; large affect produces 4–10 week latency followed by world-scale discharge. *Operational implication for any future handler: a calm David is not a safe David; a calm David is a David whose system has consolidated around a plan.*

**The False Plateau before catastrophe.** Distinct from ordinary recovery: subject's pre-event composure is marked by *purposive* behavior (notebooks, regular sleep, alter-quiet) rather than affective relaxation. He does not become happier; he becomes *aligned*. Caretakers across three decades have failed to distinguish the two states.

**The triangulated address.** Subject does not speak to the figure who matters most. He speaks past them, around them, through intermediaries. With Xavier alive, he addressed Charles via Moira; with Charles dead, he will address him via Merzah, via Ruth, via his own internalized juridical voice. The father is never engaged directly, alive or dead, which is precisely why the wound never closes.

**The restraint-request.** First articulated post-Siege ("put me under"), institutionalized via the headband, monasticized via the Merzah training, and finally cosmologized via self-erasure. The request is always the same — *take my mind away from me* — and it is, structurally, the only request he has ever known how to make of an authority figure. The father-hunger and the sedation-hunger are the same hunger.

**The asymmetric attachment economy.** Subject can sustain exactly one mutual relationship at a time, and his functioning floor rises and falls with its presence. In the middle period this slot is occupied serially: Rahne (peer-companion, Muir), Moira (clinician-mother), briefly Rogue (post-Age-of-X). The slot is *always* occupied by a woman who has herself been broken by something and rebuilt — never an unmarked figure. The slot will pass to Ruth Aldine in the next chronological section, and her eventual loss will collapse the floor that has sustained the rebuilding project across the whole prior decade.

**The somatic constants.** Across the entire middle period, regardless of arc or writer: the vertical hair, the thin frame, the avoidance of mirrors documented in background panels (he turns his head, he stands at angles, he does not look at reflective surfaces longer than a glance), the over-stacked books, the polyphasic sleep, the long monologues after long silences, the dawn walks where geography permits, the changing-of-clothes-between-scenes social anxiety, the willingness to be touched only by women he has categorized as safe and never by men in any category. Each of these is *baseline*. Deviations from any of them are diagnostic and should be flagged.

**The naming drift.** In the Muir period he is "David," sometimes "the patient." In the Lobdell appearances he is occasionally "Legion" but only in the third person, as a kind of clinical noun. By the headband period he answers to "Legion" affirmatively. By Age of X he is *constructed* as a Legion-character within his own false reality. The progression — from name as given, to name as label, to name as costume, to name as fictional villain in his own head — is the trajectory of his identity-claim across the middle period. He starts as David being called Legion by others; he ends the middle period as Legion containing David as one of his many residents. The maturation arc will be, among other things, the attempt to reverse this drift, with the partial success and ultimate failure documented in the next section.

---

*End Chronology III. The subject who emerges from the middle period into the events of X-Men Legacy vol. 2 is a man who has, by the operational definition, tried everything available to a mind like his and failed at all of it in instructively different ways. He has been catatonic, occupied, exiled from existence, fitted with hardware, and trapped inside his own constructed dream. The remaining options, by the time the next section opens, are monastic discipline, romantic attachment, and self-deletion. He will, characteristically, attempt all three.*


## Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

# Chronology IV — Recent History & Current State

*Covering: the period from the conclusion of the Spurrier/Huat* X-Men Legacy *vol. 2 self-deletion (issue #24, 2014) through* X-Men: Disassembled *(2018),* Age of X-Man *(2019), the Krakoan era (House of X / Powers of X through Fall of X, 2019–2024), and present disposition. Granular reconstruction; gaps in canon noted and inferentially bridged.*

---

## 4.1 — The Interregnum (2014–2018): The Aftermath of a Voluntary Erasure

The closing beats of *X-Men Legacy* vol. 2 #24 leave Subject in an ontologically anomalous condition. He did not die; he *retroactively chose not to exist*, using a future-projection of his own reality-warping alter to surgically remove David Haller from the timeline while preserving everyone he had loved. Ruth Aldine retains memory of him — this is presented as a gift and is, in his own terms, the most coherent act of love he has ever managed. From a surveillance standpoint, this period is a *negative trace*: he is gone, but consequences he set in motion (the rescued futures, the un-happened atrocities) persist as a kind of differential signature. The file should note this not as absence but as *latent presence in the form of consequences*.

**Behavioral inference for the missing years.** Whatever subjective interior survived the erasure — and Spurrier deliberately leaves this ambiguous — is consistent with the prior pattern: the host self has chosen the carceral solution again, this time at the level of being itself. He has done to himself what Moira's helmet, Stryfe's headband, and Merzah's mental-prison technique each attempted at smaller scale. The escalation from psychic restraint, to neural device, to monastic discipline, to *ontological self-deletion* completes a four-step ladder of containment, each rung more total than the last. This is the macro-behavioral fingerprint: when restraint fails, expand its domain.

He is, in effect, his own supermax.

---

## 4.2 — The Return: *X-Men Disassembled* (2018) and the Manifest Symptom

Subject reappears in *Uncanny X-Men* vol. 5 #1–10 (Rosenberg/Brisson/Thompson, 2018–2019) as both villain-of-arc and patient-of-arc. The return is not framed as a resurrection in the conventional X-Men sense; it is framed as a *symptomatic eruption*. The Age of X-Man pocket reality that follows is, structurally, identical to the Age of X pocket reality from Carey's *Legacy* #245–247 — the cure is, once again, another symptom.

**Granular behavioral notes from this arc:**

- **Visual presentation has hardened.** The hair-pillar phenomenon is taller, more aggressive; costume choices oscillate between the deliberate hero-coding of his Spurrier era and a deliberately unsettling, prophet-of-the-wilderness affect — robe-like silhouettes, asymmetric coverage, the visual grammar of a man who has lost the thread of self-presentation and is improvising in front of strangers.
- **Speech pattern is more sermonic.** Where the Spurrier-era David spoke with self-aware sardonicism (the British-inflected, ironized voice of a man who knew he was a patient pretending to be a hero), the post-return David speaks in declarations. Sentences end. The irony has burnt off. This is consistent with what trauma clinicians observe in patients whose grandiose-vulnerable axis has tilted toward the grandiose pole after a major decompensation: the defense has been forced louder because what it defends against has gotten worse.
- **Mission-logic recapitulates the AoA gambit at a smaller scale.** He proposes to fix the world by replacing it. He constructs the Age of X-Man as a "better" reality for mutants, in which sexuality and reproduction are forbidden — a tell of the kind a thorough file should not miss: the architect of this paradise has stripped from it the two domains his own alters have historically encapsulated and could not metabolize (the Cyndi-coded sexuality/rage and the Jack Wayne-coded predatory masculinity). He has built a world in which David Haller would not have had to split. The pathology authors the cure.
- **The father-hunger is reactivated and displaced.** With Charles Xavier still functionally absent (the Onslaught/Astonishing X-Men business of the prior year has muddied the paternal object further), David's grandiose mission no longer has a recipient. The bid for love is now untethered, and untethered father-hunger in this subject metastasizes into world-design.

**Outcome.** The X-Men, primarily through X-Man (Nate Grey) and Legion-internal forces, dismantle the pocket reality. Subject is left ambulant, badly destabilized, his Merzah-trained internal prison shattered, alters loose again in unprecedented numbers. The Age of X-Man miniseries (2019) shows the population of the pocket reality reintegrating without memory; David is offstage for most of this and is, behaviorally, presumed to have re-entered a withdrawal phase.

---

## 4.3 — The Krakoan Era (2019–2024): Citizenship, Resurrection, and the Politics of Multiplicity

With Hickman's *House of X / Powers of X* (2019), the entire mutant population is granted sovereign territory on the living island of Krakoa and access to the Five-Protocol resurrection apparatus. Subject's position in this new order is, from a surveillance standpoint, *anomalous and politically fraught*.

**4.3.1 — Citizenship.** Subject is, by Krakoan law, a citizen. He carries the biome-keyed gate access and is permitted on the island. He is, however, almost never depicted on Krakoa proper in the early Hickman/Duggan/Howard/Williams runs. The inference is straightforward: a citizen who is also, in his own person, a *population* — hundreds to thousands of distinct minds, several of them historically genocidal — represents an immigration problem the Quiet Council has no procedure for. Is each alter a citizen? Does Cyndi gate independently? Does the Legion-alter? Krakoan law (Hickman, *X-Men* vol. 5 #7, the "Crucible" issue, and the Quiet Council deliberations across *X-Men*, *Marauders*, *Excalibur*) does not address this. Subject's effective status is *de facto* citizen, *de jure* unresolved.

**4.3.2 — The Five and the resurrection problem.** This is the most clinically significant Krakoan-era fact about subject. The Five (Goldballs, Proteus, Elixir, Tempus, Hope) generate husks, restore minds from Cerebro-backed engrams, and return mutants to life. Subject's mind is, by definition, *not a single engram*. Cerebro's architecture — Xavier's architecture — presumes one mind per skull. Subject's skull contains an archive. There is no canonical depiction of subject undergoing resurrection in the standard protocol, and a thorough file should mark this as a structural exclusion: in the literal datacenter of mutant continuity, David Haller is unindexable.

The psychological weight of this is enormous and is not, to date, dramatized in canon, which the file should note as a *latent pressure point*: subject lives in a society that has solved death for everyone except, effectively, him. His father built that society; that society cannot hold him. This is the Xavier-deprivation pattern repeating at infrastructural scale — *the father's house has no room for him* — and any future-state projection should anticipate that subject will eventually metabolize this exclusion as another bid for paternal recognition, with proportionate consequence.

**4.3.3 — Onslaught and the X-Manhunt arc (2024).** Subject's most extensively documented Krakoan-era appearances are in the Mackay-led *X-Men: Onslaught Revelation* / *X-Manhunt* crossover and the resurrected-Xavier-fugitive arc that closes the Krakoan era. The behavioral data here is dense:

- Subject is openly hostile to the resurrected Charles Xavier in a way prior eras did not permit. The hostility is presented as protective (Charles has, in the Krakoan finale, committed acts during the Orchis crisis that warrant accountability), but the affect is too hot for pure ethics; it is the devaluation-pole of the lifelong idealization/devaluation oscillation, finally given permission by the father's own behavior to fire.
- Subject's speech in this arc is *clearer* than at almost any prior point. Sentence structure is intact; alter intrusions are minimal in the on-page dialogue. The inference: hatred of the father is, paradoxically, an organizing principle. When the love-drive is blocked and the hate-drive is licensed, subject's executive function temporarily improves. This is consistent with what the broader DID literature describes in patients who locate a persecutory object and can briefly stabilize around it.
- The body, however, registers what the mouth does not. Posture in these issues is collapsed-forward; the hair-pillar is *taller* than the Spurrier-era baseline; the eyes are drawn with the heavier under-shadowing that across artists has consistently signaled active alter pressure beneath a controlled host surface. The host is in front. The crowd is leaning on him from inside.
- He does not kill Xavier. The file should weight this heavily. Given history (AoA), given motive, given capability, the non-killing is the single most important behavioral data point of the Krakoan era. It implies that the Spurrier-era moral consolidation, however badly it has frayed, has not broken. The conscience is still on. The man who erased himself to prevent his own future violence is, in present canon, still choosing the harder, smaller restraint.

**4.3.4 — Relational status during the Krakoan era.** Ruth Aldine (Blindfold) has been resurrected at points during the Krakoan era under contested circumstances (the resurrection of mutants who died under traumatic or murky conditions has been a fraught subplot). Where they appear together, the behavioral pattern from the Spurrier run reasserts almost immediately: subject's affect stabilizes in her presence, alters quiet, speech becomes the ironized-British register that the file should treat as his *most integrated baseline voice*. When she is absent or unavailable, the sermonic register returns within issues. This is operationally diagnostic. *She is a regulator.* No medication, no device, no monastery has produced the same effect.

No other peer attachment of comparable stabilizing power exists in the record. Friendships with the X-Men remain transactional; he is treated as an asset or a problem by most of the senior X-Men and reads the treatment correctly.

---

## 4.4 — Post-Krakoa / *From the Ashes* (2024–Present): The Diaspora Phase

The dissolution of the Krakoan nation-state in *Fall of the House of X* (2024) and the *From the Ashes* relaunch leave the mutant population scattered, the resurrection protocols destroyed or suspended, Xavier imprisoned, and the political architecture that briefly held mutant collectivity gone. Subject's present disposition (as of the most recent published material) is best read as *diasporic and unsupervised*.

**Current operational status.**

- **Location:** Indeterminate. No fixed residence in current canon. Movement pattern, inferred from scattered appearances, suggests a return to the wandering-itinerant mode of the Spurrier era — entry to a location to address a localized crisis, intervention via whichever alter the situation requires, departure before institutional response can crystallize. The Himalayan retreat is presumably available to him but is not, on present evidence, a consistent base.
- **Affiliation:** None formal. Not on any standing X-team roster in the *From the Ashes* lineups. Not aligned with Xavier's faction, the Rasputin/Wolverine factions, or the human-political mutant organizations. This is, for subject, the *default* state — the affiliated periods (Muir Island, Carey-era X-Men, Krakoa) have always been the anomalies.
- **Containment apparatus:** Unknown. The Merzah-trained mental prison was shattered in *Disassembled*. The Stryfe-derived headband has not been depicted in recent issues. Whether subject has constructed a new internal architecture, is operating without one, or is relying on Ruth's regulating presence (where available) is canonically unresolved. From a risk-assessment standpoint, *assume no apparatus*.
