The costume itself, when designed in the Spurrier era, is selected to be *visibly his* — a public declaration of singular identity that an observer should read as compensatory, not vain.

**Reading.** Reads at night, in bed, lying on his back with the book held overhead — an exhausting posture that he maintains for hours. Marks pages by folding the corners; never uses bookmarks. Underlines in pencil. Underlines passages about fathers, about sons, about wholeness, about kings.

**Speech to himself.** Increases at dawn and at dusk. The two liminal times of day are documented across multiple runs as periods when alter-chatter is most audible to outside observers.

---

## X. SUMMARY OF THE FASTEST READS

For an observer needing rapid state-assessment at distance:

- *Hair amplitude:* tracks psionic load, not emotional load — the relationship between load and emotion is non-linear.
- *Shoulder asymmetry (left up):* acute social stress; alter activity rising.
- *Backward half-step with left foot:* threat-state engaged, regardless of orientation.
- *Whispered exchanges with no interlocutor:* low to moderate dissociative activity, host still in charge.
- *Mid-sentence pause with slow blink:* alter-switch imminent (15–40s).
- *Closed eyes + small nod before discharge:* controlled deployment.
- *Wide eyes + open jaw before discharge:* catastrophic deployment — clear the area.
- *Post-victory hair-rise + proposing a larger operation:* refuse the proposal; isolate the subject; the rage-alters are bidding.
- *"I'm not — I was never —" sentence-fragment:* identity-level defeat; ninety-day crisis window opens now.
- *Vocabulary inflating under stress (four-syllable words where one would do):* reaction formation; the system is destabilizing under the speech.
- *Hot shower, second of the day:* the load is unusually high and he is self-medicating somatically.
- *Code-switch into childhood Hebrew or French:* he is with a person he trusts at the deepest level available to him; document the listener.
- *Lying on the floor rather than the bed:* acute grief, hours one through seventy-two.
- *Project announcement, large and structured around a lost figure:* sublimation phase of grief beginning; the catastrophic act is between three and twelve weeks away.

The reliability of these reads degrades in inverse proportion to the host self's presence in the body. When the host is absent, the alter forward has its


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# UATU DOSSIER — SECTION 7
## DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

*Subject: David Charles Haller*
*Compiled from longitudinal behavioral observation, cross-referenced against in-panel domestic scenes (New Mutants #26–28; X-Men Legacy vol. 1 #245–250, vol. 2 #1–24), and inferred from established somatic, psychiatric, and operational baselines.*

---

### 7.1 — Preliminary Note on the Difficulty of Surveilling This Subject

Most surveillance subjects produce a stable signal: the same coffee shop at 7:42, the same route home, the same partner, the same Sunday call to a mother. A field officer's job is to find the deviation in a baseline. Subject Haller inverts this entirely. The baseline *is* deviation. The body that leaves the room at 0800 is not always the body, by behavioral metric, that returns at 1700 — and on bad days is not the same body at 0830. A surveillance team assigned to him would, within seventy-two hours, file a report indicating that they were tracking what appeared to be three to six unrelated individuals occupying the same somatic shell on different schedules, and would ask for clarification.

The genuine signal — the *host's* signal, the David-underneath signal — is recoverable but requires the analyst to subtract out the alter-traffic. What follows is that subtracted record: the routines, rituals, and habits-of-place that belong to the host self, with notation of where alter-incursion typically intrudes, and what a week of disciplined observation would actually record on a clipboard.

---

### 7.2 — Sleep, Waking, and the First Hour

Subject sleeps poorly and has done so since the catatonia broke in 1985. Surveillance of any of his known habitations (the Muir Island annex in the late 1980s; the various X-Mansion guest rooms in the 1990s and 2000s; the Himalayan cell at Merzah's monastery; the bedsit-style rented rooms across Europe and Asia in the Spurrier-era walkabout phase) reveals the same architecture of night: a delayed sleep onset of typically ninety minutes to three hours, fragmented mid-cycle wakings between 0230 and 0430, and a final compressed sleep terminating between 0530 and 0700 regardless of what time was set on the alarm. He keeps no alarm. He does not need one. His mind wakes him before any external prompt can.

The room itself shows a consistent signature: minimal furnishing, blankets kicked to the floor or twisted at the foot of the bed (motor activity in REM is high, consistent with the trauma profile), and — invariably — at least one light left on. Subject does not sleep in full dark. Field observation across decades confirms this without exception. A bedside lamp, a hallway light bleeding under the door, the screen of a paperback's reading clip — something. Inferred cause: the astral plane is dark, and in the dark the alters are louder. Light is a soft restraint.

He does not check a phone on waking. He has owned phones — Spurrier-era panels show him with one — but he distrusts the device, holds it at arm's length, treats it as an object that arrives at him from outside rather than an appendage. The first conscious act after waking is, near-universally, to sit up on the edge of the bed and put both feet flat on the floor for thirty to ninety seconds, hands on knees, eyes closed. This is not meditation. It is *triage*. He is taking inventory of who is in the room with him today.

Hair: vertical on waking and vertical throughout the day. He does not flatten it; flattening does not hold. Observers consistently report that the upward set re-establishes within minutes of any attempt to brush it down. This is autonomic, not stylistic, and a useful biometric — when the hair *does* lie flat for any extended period it indicates either a deep alter-takeover (Jack Wayne grooms; the host does not) or a state of true psionic exhaustion.

---

### 7.3 — Morning Ritual: The Headcount

Following the floor-sitting, the host engages in what monks at the Himalayan retreat documented as his "morning naming," and which appears in muted form in every habitation since. He names them. Out loud, or in clearly subvocalized lip-movement, he says the names of the loudest alters of the previous night and tells them, in effect, to wait their turn. Surveillance microphones in the monastery period recorded fragments — *not yet, not today, stay where I put you* — addressed to no visible interlocutor. To an untrained observer this would log as psychotic self-talk. It is in fact a maintenance ritual of considerable sophistication, the verbal analog of a prison guard walking the cellblock and confirming each cell is locked.

Breakfast, when he eats it, is small and texturally simple: toast, plain rice, tea. He prefers tea to coffee, established consistently from Muir Island onward (British/Scottish institutional habituation; the mother's diplomatic-European household reinforced rather than disrupted it). Coffee, when he drinks it, generally indicates Jack Wayne is forward — Wayne drinks coffee, often black, and the subject's espresso intake is a reliable surveillance proxy for that alter's emergence. The handle of any teacup he uses shows a characteristic grip wear-pattern consistent with two-handed cradling rather than one-handed holding; he warms his hands on the cup. He is, somatically, almost always slightly cold.

He does not eat with others if he can avoid it. Communal mealtimes at the X-Mansion are documented as a stressor; he ate fast, ate early, ate late, ate in his room, or ate not at all. In the Spurrier-era flats he ate standing at a counter.

---

### 7.4 — Dress, Object Habits, and the Question of the Costume

Wardrobe across decades is consistent in palette and structure: dark trousers or jeans, plain shirts (often long-sleeved regardless of climate; the arms are a vulnerable somatic site for him, perhaps related to the body-image instability documented in §2), and a coat or jacket that is too heavy for the weather. He overdresses. This is the host's preference. When the wardrobe abruptly *correctifies* to the climate — short sleeves on a hot day, lighter weight — an alter is driving.

The costume he designed for himself in the Spurrier era is treated by the host with a private, almost embarrassed care. He folds it. He keeps it in a single piece of luggage. He puts it on only when he has decided in advance to act as a hero, never as default wear, and never casually. Putting it on is itself a ritual: he tends to do it standing, slowly, in front of a reflective surface he is *not* looking into directly. He looks at the costume, not at himself wearing it. This is consistent with the documented mirror-avoidance (§5, pressure points): he can tolerate his image as long as it is mediated by the role.

Pocket inventory, when documented in panel or inferable from behavior: a small notebook (he writes; more on this below), a pen, occasionally a folded photograph (a child-era image of his mother; one period included a photograph of Xavier, which he carried for months and eventually burned in a ritual that took most of an evening), and a worry-object of some kind — a stone, a string of monastery beads from the Himalayan period, a coin. He works the object with his fingers when alters are pressing. Field observation would track this hand-activity as a primary tell: increased bead-working or stone-rolling correlates with imminent switch.

---

### 7.5 — Work Patterns

"Work," for this subject, has no consistent vocational shape. He has been, at various points, a patient, a ward, an unofficial student, a probationary X-Man, a monastic novice, an itinerant freelance hero, and a homeless wanderer. There is no career arc; there is only the *project*, which is always either the integration of the self or the completion of the father's dream, and which absorbs all energy that a more ordinary subject would distribute across employment.

What he does with his hours, however, is patterned.

**Reading.** Daily, multiple hours. Subject is bookish and has been since childhood (the diplomat-mother's house was a literary one). Preference is for philosophy, theology, history (particularly histories of the twentieth century, which he reads compulsively and which agitate him predictably — the Holocaust material is unavoidable for him and never neutral), and, in the Spurrier era, mass-market paperback fiction picked up at train stations. He underlines. He folds page corners. He does not write in margins; he writes in the notebook. He returns to certain books many times: *The Brothers Karamazov* has been observed in his possession across at least three eras, and the volume he carried in the Spurrier walkabout was visibly broken at the spine in the section containing the Grand Inquisitor.

**Writing.** Daily, brief. The notebook is not a journal in the conventional sense. It is a logbook of alter activity, a list of names and times, with occasional sentences directed at the alters or at the absent father. The handwriting is *not consistent* — it changes within entries, sometimes within sentences, as different alters take the pen. This is one of the most clinically diagnostic artifacts a search of his belongings would produce. The host writes in a small, leftward-slanted, controlled hand. Cyndi prints in large, jagged capitals, often pressing through the page. Jack Wayne writes in a confident, looping cursive that he treats as more legible than it is. Karami writes in a careful, slightly archaic English with occasional Arabic phrasing. A forensic document examiner would, on first encountering the notebook, classify it as the product of four or more individuals collaborating on a single artifact.

**Training.** Subject practices — and this has intensified post-Himalayan period — a daily regimen of mental exercise that is functionally meditative but is, in his own framing, *carceral architecture.* He spends one to three hours per day rebuilding the internal prison. This is not pleasant for him. Observers report he emerges from these sessions visibly drained, sometimes nauseated, occasionally with minor nosebleeds (a recurring somatic signature of psionic overuse, documented from the Claremont era onward). Time-of-day for this is variable but trends toward late morning, after the headcount has stabilized and before the afternoon alter-activity rises.

**Patrol / heroic activity (Spurrier era and intermittent).** When the costume is on, the work is episodic, mission-driven, and almost never coordinated with the institutional X-effort. He picks targets idiosyncratically — small injustices, often, deliberately chosen *under* the scale of his ability, as a sort of grandiosity-management exercise. He is trying to learn to be a hero at the volume of a person and not at the volume of a god. Field observation would catch him doing things like intervening in domestic abuse cases in residential buildings, then leaving without identifying himself. The work is real. The discipline of working small is also real, and harder for him than the work itself.

---

### 7.6 — Downtime

Downtime is the most dangerous part of his day, and he knows it. Unstructured time invites alter-traffic. He has developed compensatory rituals.

**Walking.** Long, solitary, often without a destination. He walks for hours. In urban environments he tends to follow water — rivers, canals, harbor edges. Inferred soothing function: the sound of moving water is a documented dissociation-floor in trauma populations, and the open horizon line over water reduces the claustrophobic interior pressure of the alter-population. Surveillance of any week would log substantial unaccounted ambulatory hours along whatever water-feature was nearest his lodging.

**Music.** He listens, alone, with what appear to be cheap over-ear headphones. Taste fragments by alter: the host favors quiet acoustic material and certain pieces of liturgical and minimalist composition; Cyndi takes the headphones for punk and aggressive electronic; Jack Wayne prefers older rock and certain forms of jazz. The headphones are themselves a containment device — he uses them to give the alters their own audio channel without letting it bleed into shared social space.

**Sitting.** He can sit for very long periods. Not meditatively — just sit. On benches, on stone walls, on the edges of fountains, on the floor of his own room with his back against the bed-frame. Observers would log this as one of the most consistent baseline behaviors: David Haller, motionless, looking at something middle-distance, for forty to ninety minutes at a stretch, several times a week.

**Sketching.** Documented intermittently. He draws faces. The faces are sometimes recognizable as alters; sometimes as Xavier; sometimes as no one identifiable. He does not consider himself an artist and does not show the work.

---

### 7.7 — Social Contact and the Rhythm of the Week

Subject is functionally solitary by default. A surveillance log of an ordinary week would record:

- Zero or one substantive in-person conversations with non-clinical/non-mission contacts.
- One to three brief transactional encounters (shopkeepers, monks, landlords, the occasional sympathetic stranger; he is unfailingly polite in these, almost formal, a residue of the diplomatic-household upbringing).
- During periods of attachment to Ruth Aldine: daily contact, often extended, often non-verbal — sitting together, walking together, eating together. His rhythm during these periods is measurably more regular: he sleeps better, eats more, drinks less coffee (i.e., Jack Wayne is suppressed by genuine intimacy), writes less in the notebook.
- During periods without Ruth or an equivalent: the week is functionally indistinguishable from solitude. He may go six to ten days without speaking to anyone at length.

He does not initiate phone calls. He answers them when they come. He does not use social media in any era where it has been available. He resists being photographed and tends to step out of frame when cameras appear, a behavior consistent both with surveillance-aversion and with the deeper mirror-aversion already noted.

---

### 7.8 — Habits of Place

Across every habitation he has occupied, certain spatial patterns recur and would be flagged by any competent residential surveillance.

- **He claims a corner.** In any room, he gravitates to a corner seat with sightlines to the door. This is the trauma profile; it is also the practical caution of a man whose own body can be hijacked and who would prefer to see whoever is coming.
- **He leaves doors ajar.** Fully closed doors he experiences as confining; fully open doors he experiences as exposed. The ajar position is his compromise and is observable across decades.
- **He maps exits.** On entering any new space he is observed to perform a brief, almost imperceptible scan — eyes to each doorway, each window. This is rapid and could easily be missed; it is, however, consistent.
- **He returns to the same outdoor sites.** A bench, a particular curve of a riverwalk, a specific tree. He establishes a small repertoire of three to five outdoor locations near any lodging and rotates among them. Field officers would identify these within four days and could reliably intercept him at one of them within seven.
- **He avoids crowds.** Markets, plazas, transit hubs at peak hour. He moves through public space at off-hours, takes early trains, walks at dawn or after dusk. The crowds amplify telepathic bleed and stress the alter-containment; he has learned this experientially.

---

### 7.9 — Substance Use, Appetite, and the Body

Negligible. Subject does not drink to intoxication (Jack Wayne does; the host does not, and the host has, with some difficulty, learned to refuse drinks pressed on him because Wayne would take them). He does not use recreational drugs. He smoked, briefly, in one documented Spurrier-era panel sequence, and the smoking appeared to be Cyndi's behavior rather than his own.

Appetite is modest. The body is thin (§2) and stays thin. He forgets to eat. He has had to set rules for himself about eating at fixed times because the host self does not reliably register hunger — the interoceptive signal is one of many things drowned by the alter-noise. Caloric intake on a typical day is, by inference, under maintenance.

Hydration is better; he drinks tea and water steadily through the day. This appears to be a learned compensation.

Sexual behavior: largely absent from the record except in the Ruth attachment, where it is implied as part of an ordinary intimate partnership. Sexuality is one of the affects the alter-system encapsulates — Jack Wayne carries it; Cyndi carries a different, more chaotic version of it — and the host's own erotic life is, by all evidence, quiet, anxious, and conducted only within attachments he trusts to a degree he extends to almost no one.

---

### 7.10 — A Composite Week of Surveillance

If an officer were assigned a seven-day continuous watch on the subject during a stable period (Spurrier-era, post-monastery, host integrated, no current crisis), the log would read approximately as follows:

**Monday.** Wakes 0612. Sits on edge of bed 47 seconds. Bathroom, tea. Notebook entry, 4 minutes. Reads 91 minutes. Walks to riverbank bench (Site A), 38 minutes outbound. Sits, 64 minutes. Walks to a bakery, purchases bread and a piece of fruit, eats half. Returns to lodging. Mental training session, ~110 minutes, emerges pale. Naps, 40 minutes. Reads. Listens to headphones. Eats small dinner, alone. Writes briefly. Lights out 2340; sleep onset estimated 0115.

**Tuesday.** Similar to Monday, with one substitution: in late afternoon, an apparent alter-incursion lasting approximately 22 minutes during which the subject visited a different cafe than his usual, ordered coffee (black), spoke with apparent ease to a stranger at the next table, then abruptly stood, looked confused, paid, and left. Returned to lodging visibly shaken. Extra notebook time that night.

**Wednesday.** Costume day. Subject dressed in costume at 1430 after extended preparation. Departed via fire escape rather than front door. Was off-grid for 6 hours 12 minutes. Returned in costume, changed, ate substantially for the first time in three days, slept hard from 2210 to 0530.

**Thursday.** Low day. Spent most of daylight hours in the room. Reading and sitting. One walk, short, to Site B. Did not eat dinner.

**Friday.** Long walk along water, 4+ hours. Sat at Site C for over an hour at dusk. Met no one. Returned, called Ruth (if applicable to period) or attempted and abandoned a call.

**Saturday.** Better day. Two meals. Sketching observed through window. Notebook activity light, indicating low alter pressure. Mental training session shorter than usual.

**Sunday.** Variable. If the day's news contained reference to Charles Xavier, mutant politics, the Holocaust, or any historical atrocity, expect destabilization, increased ambulatory hours, possible alter-incursion, and a difficult night. If the day's news was quiet, expect a repeat of Saturday's pattern.

Across the week: approximately 35–50 ambulatory hours outside the lodging; 20–30 hours reading; 8–15 hours of mental training; 2–4 hours of substantive social contact (or zero, in solitary phases); 35–45 hours of sleep (well under the population norm); and an unknowable but nonzero number of hours during which the body was present but the host was not.

---

### 7.11 — Summary

The daily life of this subject is, properly understood, the daily life of a man running a small institution out of his own skull. The routines are not eccentricities. They are the operating procedures of an under-resourced facility with violent inmates, a single guard who is himself one of the inmates, and no external oversight. Every habit catalogued here — the lit room at night, the corner seat, the headcount, the notebook, the water-walks, the deliberate small heroism, the cradle-grip on the teacup — is a load-bearing element. Remove any one and the facility degrades measurably within days.

A surveillance team that understood this would stop trying to find the deviation and start logging the maintenance. The maintenance *is* the life. The subject is, on any given Tuesday, performing an act of structural self-preservation that most human beings will never be asked to perform on any day of their lives, and he is doing it while making tea.

— END SECTION 7 —


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

> *"Every person you meet, you measure against a ghost. Your ghost has a bald head and a wheelchair and he never quite looked at you."* — internal voice attributed to Subject during the Spurrier-era monastic sequences; consistent with affect logged across thirty years of canon.

The family map is small. Subject has, by blood, exactly two known parents and one half-brother of consequence; by absorption, one dead terrorist who lives inside his skull; by surrogacy, two parental figures (MacTaggert, Merzah) whose taxonomy properly belongs here rather than under mentors because Subject *files them* internally as parents. We include them. We also include the dead — Daniel Shomron, the unnamed embassy attackers, the Karami fragment, and the spectral post-mortem Charles Xavier — because for this Subject the dead are not retired from the relational system; they remain online, accruing leverage, modifying behavior, and in two cases occupying psychic real estate as functional alters or quasi-alters.

The unifying behavioral observation across the entire family field: **Subject does not have relationships with people; he has relationships with their absence.** The living are filtered through the template of who has *withdrawn* — physically, emotionally, or by death. The dead are managed as if still capable of withdrawing further. This produces, in every dyad below, the same gravitational distortion: the closer the bond, the more catastrophically Subject performs for an audience that is not in the room.

---

## 1. CHARLES FRANCIS XAVIER — Father (biological, withheld, idealized, killed by Subject's hand, posthumously deified)

**History.** Xavier conceived Subject during the Haifa period with Gabrielle Haller, departed without disclosure, and did not learn of Subject's existence until Moira MacTaggert presented an already-catatonic adolescent on Muir Island (New Mutants #26–28). The biological fact preceded the relational fact by roughly a decade and a half. When relational contact began, it began with Xavier as *clinician* to Subject as *patient* — a frame neither of them ever fully escaped, and which poisoned every subsequent attempt at a father-son register.

**Phase 1 — Pre-contact (childhood, Haifa/Paris).** Subject does not know who his father is. The absence is structured rather than acknowledged; Gabrielle does not lie but she also does not name. Subject grows up under the silhouette of a man whose shape he cannot see. The behavioral residue here is critical for the later record: Subject learned, before he had language for it, that his father was *withheld on purpose by adults who loved him*. This is the originating model for every later interpersonal scenario — love and concealment are the same operation.

**Phase 2 — Recognition (Muir Island, post-catatonia).** Xavier identifies as father in the Claremont arc. The disclosure does not produce the catharsis the panel-grammar wants you to expect. Subject responds with a kind of stunned formality — body language tracked across the original Sienkiewicz pages shows Subject *physically receding* from Xavier even while his face composes itself into politeness. This is the first observable instance of a behavior that will become signature: **the more emotionally significant the encounter, the more Subject's body backs away while his face advances.** Xavier, for his part, behaves with the careful overcorrection of a man who has just been handed a moral debt he cannot afford to itemize.

**Phase 3 — Idealization and pursuit (mid-Claremont through Lobdell).** Once activated, Subject's father-hunger becomes the organizing force of his cognition. He studies Xavier's published dream the way another child would study a parent's face — not for comprehension but for clues about how to be loved. The dream is metabolized as homework. By the time of the Legion Quest, Subject has constructed a fully articulated theory: *Xavier wants Magneto neutralized; if I neutralize Magneto in the past, I will have given my father the world he wanted; he will love me; everything will resolve.* This is not impulse. The plan was researched, sequenced, and timed. The pathology is in the premise (love is purchasable through atrocity), not the execution.

**Phase 4 — Patricide (X-Men #320–321, Uncanny #320, 1995).** Subject's psi-blade, intended for Magneto, takes Xavier instead. The Age of Apocalypse follows. Subject is erased in the reset. For our purposes the salient fact is that **Subject committed the act believing it was love.** No subsequent retelling, by any writer, has him recanting the *motive*; he recants only the outcome. The act remains, in his internal accounting, a love-act that miscarried — which is a far more destabilizing thing to live with than a hate-act that succeeded.

**Phase 5 — Post-resurrection coexistence (Mackie/Carey).** Subject and Xavier inhabit the same institution again, with the killing notionally undone by cosmological reset. Neither references it directly. Observed micro-pattern in Carey's Legacy panels: Subject does not enter a room Xavier is already in; he waits in the hallway until Xavier exits, then takes the space. When forced into shared scenes, he stands at the spatial periphery, body angled forty-five degrees away, eye contact rationed in fractions of a second. Xavier reciprocates by overusing Subject's first name — a tell of a man performing intimacy he has not earned.

**Phase 6 — Death of Xavier (Avengers vs. X-Men, 2012) and the Spurrier reckoning.** Xavier's killing by Cyclops removes the only entity in the cosmos with whom Subject's central drive could potentially be resolved. The reaction is not grief in the conventional sense; it is **commission**. Subject reads the death as an inheritance, a deathbed instruction he must execute. The entire Spurrier run is the working-out of this inheritance: the costume (self-designed, deliberately *not* X-uniform), the monastic training, the refusal of the school, the construction of a freelance heroism that rejects Xavier's institutions while completing Xavier's mission. This is a textbook bereavement displacement — Subject cannot mourn a father he killed, so he becomes the father instead, badly.

**Phase 7 — Hauntology (Spurrier through present).** Xavier persists as an internalized voice, occasionally manifesting as quasi-alter, occasionally as projected hallucination, occasionally as conscience. Subject converses with him. The Xavier-imago is, observably, a *worse* father than the historical Xavier — more demanding, more withholding, more disappointed. Subject has populated his interior with a paternal figure calibrated to *guarantee* failure. This is consistent with the vulnerable-narcissistic structure: the internalized parent is a perpetual-deficit machine, and the Subject's heroism is the perpetual interest payment.

**Dynamics.** Asymmetrical and clinical-coded from the first encounter. Xavier could not stop being a telepath observing a damaged mind; Subject could not stop being a damaged mind observed by his father. Neither permitted the other a register of ordinary affection.

**Trust/Safety.** Zero. Subject *idealizes* Xavier, which is distinct from trusting him; trust requires the possibility of disappointment without catastrophe, and Subject has never offered Xavier disappointment without catastrophe.

**Failure mode.** Annihilatory. When the bond fails, it fails at the scale of a timeline.

**Leverage.** Total, posthumously. Anyone capable of credibly speaking *as* Xavier, or *for* Xavier, or *about* what Xavier would have wanted, can move Subject several steps in any direction. The Spurrier-era Merzah exploits this knowingly; numerous adversaries have exploited it less skillfully.

---

## 2. GABRIELLE HALLER — Mother (Holocaust survivor, diplomat, the parent who stayed)

**History.** Subject's mother is the parent who did not abandon him, did not withhold, did not lie about her own identity, did not die in the embassy attack, did not later become his god, did not later become his victim. By every reasonable metric she is the healthier object in the family field. She is also, in the canon record and in Subject's behavior toward her, **the relationship Subject neglects most.** This is significant. The parent who stays receives less of Subject's attention than the parent who left, the parent who died, and the parent who was a terrorist Subject mind-killed when he was nine.

**Phase 1 — Childhood Haifa/Paris.** Gabrielle raises Subject in a multilingual diplomat's household marked by the visible aftermath of her own survivorship. She is loving in a register Subject can register as *love*, but the household has the texture of a survivor's household: emotional reserve calibrated against worse possibilities, gratitude held just above grief, the everyday slightly evacuated. Subject learns from her, before he learns it from any other source, that an adult can be wholly present and also unreachable. He generalizes this into a lifelong template.

**Phase 2 — The embassy attack.** Daniel Shomron — Gabrielle's partner, Subject's stepfather-in-effect — is murdered in front of Subject. Subject's catastrophic psionic response follows. Gabrielle is in the building, survives, and is the first adult to confront the aftermath. Inferred from the consistent characterization across retellings: she does not blame him. She also does not ask him to talk about it. What is offered is *managed silence*. This is the wrong intervention for the trauma, and it is the intervention a Holocaust-survivor mother of her generation would, predictably and forgivably, default to. The resulting confusion in Subject is durable: his mother neither punishes nor processes the worst thing he has ever done, which leaves him with no model for how to do either.

**Phase 3 — Catatonia and Muir Island handoff.** Gabrielle, unable to manage Subject's deterioration, transfers him to Moira MacTaggert's care. Whether this was abandonment or pragmatism depends entirely on perspective; in Subject's psychic accounting it likely registers as both, the ambivalence unresolved. She visits — the visits are referenced in Claremont and Carey — but the maternal function effectively passes to MacTaggert at this point.

**Phase 4 — Adult periodic contact.** Across the entire post-Muir record, Gabrielle appears intermittently. Subject calls. Subject visits. Subject does not stay long. The pattern is consistent: contact is initiated by Subject during periods of relative stability, terminated by Subject at the first sign of deterioration. He shields her. He also keeps her out of the parts of his life that matter most. She has not, in the published record, met Ruth Aldine in any meaningful way; she was not consulted about the Spurrier-era heroic turn; she did not learn of his self-erasure until after.

**Dynamics.** Affectionate, formal, anglo-polite in its English register and Hebrew-formal in its other one. Subject reverts, in her presence, to a more juvenile speech pattern — observable in any flashback or visit panel where dialogue is given. He becomes physically smaller in her vicinity: shoulders rounded, hair slightly less vertical (the psionic discharge attenuates around her — a somatic *tell* that she has a calming effect his conscious mind refuses to credit her for).

**Trust/Safety.** High in absolute terms, low in operational terms. He trusts her completely; he confides in her almost nothing. This is the paradox of the "good-enough" parent of a catastrophically damaged child: the safety is real and the safety is unusable, because using it would require admitting the damage in terms the parent cannot help carry.

**Failure mode.** Withdrawal. Subject does not fight with his mother; he disappears from her, sometimes for years.

**Leverage.** Significant and almost never exploited in canon. A threat to Gabrielle would dismantle Subject's already minimal regulatory capacity; the fact that this lever has been pulled so rarely is itself a clue that adversaries who know him well *understand it is the lever he would not survive having pulled*, and pulling it removes Subject from the board entirely rather than moving him on it.

---

## 3. DAVID CHARLES HALLER (the host self) — Internal Bond with Self

**History.** It is appropriate, in a Subject of this structure, to treat the host self as a relational object in its own right. The host — the personality that bears the legal name, that signs documents, that appears in most family photographs — is one personality among many, and the relationship between this personality and the rest of the system is the central interior bond of the dossier.

**Phase 1 — Pre-Karami childhood.** A unified self of some kind existed up to age eight-to-ten. The behavioral record of this self is sparse but consistent: bright child, multilingual, attached to mother and stepfather, no documented behavioral abnormalities. This child died in the embassy.

**Phase 2 — Post-attack, pre-Muir.** The host is reorganized around the Karami absorption. He is no longer the only voice. He does not know this. He thinks the voices are thoughts. The interval between the attack and the catatonia is the period in which the multiplicity is *settling*, like sediment in disturbed water, and the host's first relationship with the system is one of confusion mistaken for normalcy.

**Phase 3 — Catatonia.** Host offline. The system runs without him.

**Phase 4 — Reintegration as host-among-equals.** The post-Claremont David is not the original child restored; he is a *new* host built on the chassis of the original, with explicit knowledge that he is one of many. His relationship with the rest of the system is, accordingly, the relationship of a constitutional monarch with a hostile parliament. He nominally presides; he is regularly overruled.

**Phase 5 — The carceral phase (Spurrier).** Host, with Merzah's training, attempts to imprison the other alters inside a literal mental prison. This is the relationship-with-self at its most pathological: the host has decided that the only governable relationship with the rest of his personality is incarceration. The arc dramatizes this as failing in exactly the way actual carceral systems fail — the prisoners escape, the prison corrupts the warden, the warden becomes one of the things he was containing.

**Phase 6 — Self-deletion.** The terminal act of the relationship-with-self is the host's decision that the system as a whole — including himself — must be erased to prevent foreseen harm. This is the only act in the entire dossier in which the host acts *on behalf of* the system rather than against it. It is, accordingly, both the most ethically coherent act of Subject's life and the most self-annihilating.

**Dynamics.** Internal-civil-war model. The host's authority over the body is contingent, contestable, and frequently lost. He negotiates. He bargains. He bullies. He pleads.

**Trust/Safety.** None. The host cannot trust his own continuity from one hour to the next.

**Failure mode.** Eviction. When the host fails, an alter takes the body.

**Leverage.** Whoever can speak to the host *as a self worth preserving* — Ruth has done this; Merzah did it instrumentally; Xavier never managed it — gains influence the alters cannot easily override.

---

## 4. JEMAIL KARAMI — Absorbed Consciousness (the dead terrorist who became the most adult voice in Subject's head)

**History.** Karami was the lead attacker in the embassy assault, killed by Subject's psionic counter-strike, and absorbed at the moment of death into Subject's mind. He is therefore not, strictly, an alter generated by Subject's psyche; he is a *real other person* whose consciousness now resides inside Subject. This makes the bond categorically different from the other internal relationships and warrants its own entry under family — because for Subject, Karami functions as something between an uncle, a confessor, and an estranged twin.

**Phase 1 — Initial hostility.** Karami, on becoming aware of his situation, attempts to dominate the host. He fails. Over years of cohabitation he undergoes — and this is the canon record, not interpretation — a moral evolution: exposure to Subject's memories, particularly to Gabrielle's Holocaust testimony and to the texture of Jewish life in the household, leads Karami to revise his prior ideological commitments. He becomes the most reflective and integrated of the alters.

**Phase 2 — Reintegration attempts.** In the original New Mutants arc, Karami achieves a partial merger with the host, effectively dissolving his separateness in service of stabilizing Subject. He recurs, however; alters in this Subject's system do not stay dissolved.

**Phase 3 — Recurrent advisory function.** Across decades, Karami is the voice that appears in Subject's interior monologue when ethical clarity is required. He is, perversely, the closest thing Subject has to a functioning superego — a man Subject killed at age nine, who has spent the intervening decades inside Subject's head atoning by being good to him.

**Dynamics.** Reparative on Karami's side, dependent on Subject's. The relationship has an asymmetric moral weight: Karami owes Subject his death and his second life; Subject owes Karami the only consistent ethical counsel he has ever received.

**Trust/Safety.** The highest of any internal relationship. Karami is the one voice in Subject's head he can reliably believe is not himself rationalizing.

**Failure mode.** Karami is overridable by the more powerful alters; when affect spikes past a certain threshold, his voice goes inaudible.

**Leverage.** Whoever can engage Karami directly — and a sufficiently skilled telepath could — would acquire access to Subject's most stable judgment, bypassing both the host and the more dangerous alters.

---

## 5. DANIEL SHOMRON — Stepfather (murdered in front of Subject; foundational ghost)

**History.** Shomron was Gabrielle's partner, the man functioning as father in the household during the years Xavier was absent, and the adult male whose murder constitutes the originating trauma of the entire dossier. He is dead in panel one of Subject's psychic life. Everything else is the aftermath.

**The relationship Subject *had* with him before death.** Sparse but tonally consistent across retellings: warm, ordinary, present. Shomron was *good to him*. This matters because it means the loss is not contaminated by ambivalence; the love was uncomplicated, the murder was unambiguous, and Subject has nowhere to file the rage except onto himself (for failing to prevent it) and onto the attackers (one of whom now lives inside him).

**The relationship Subject *has* with him after death.** Shomron does not exist as an alter — he was not absorbed — but he exists as a persistent absent referent. In Subject's interior, Shomron occupies the position of *the father who was actually fathering me when I lost everything*. Xavier, when later introduced, is measured against this prior, dead, better father, and Xavier loses the comparison every time. This is a significant and underdiscussed feature of the Xavier dynamic: the biological father is competing posthumously with a dead stepfather who never abandoned, never withheld, and never had the opportunity to disappoint.

**Dynamics.** Idealization in absentia. The dead Shomron is permitted to be a perfect father in Subject's interior because he is not present to fall short.

**Trust/Safety.** Inapplicable to a corpse, except as benchmark.

**Failure mode.** Already failed, catastrophically, before Subject had language for it.

**Leverage.** Anyone invoking Shomron's name credibly — and very few characters in canon know it — would access affect in Subject that has been kept frozen since age nine, and the unfreezing would be uncontrollable.

---

## 6. MOIRA MacTAGGERT — Surrogate Mother / Primary Clinician

**History.** MacTaggert accepts Subject onto Muir Island during the catatonic phase, becomes his primary caregiver and clinician for years, and continues in that combined role intermittently across the Claremont and post-Claremont runs. She is, functionally, the parent who *raised the post-trauma Subject* — Gabrielle raised the pre-trauma child; MacTaggert raised the patient.

**Dynamics.** Dual-role and structurally compromised. MacTaggert was simultaneously mother-figure and treating physician, a dual relationship that no clinical ethics framework permits but that the comics premise required. The result is that Subject cannot cleanly love her without also resenting her authority over his treatment, and cannot cleanly resent her authority without feeling he is hurting his mother. This bind is never resolved.

**Trust/Safety.** Moderate-high in the maternal register, complicated by her clinical knowledge of him. She knows things about him no one else knows, and the knowledge cuts both ways.

**Failure mode.** MacTaggert's own arcs — her son Proteus, her own death, her later cosmological revelations — repeatedly pull her out of the maternal availability Subject requires. He does not blame her; he is observed to react to her unavailability by deterioration, which is the body's accusation his speech refuses.

**Leverage.** Substantial. MacTaggert is the only person who has ever held Subject's clinical record in something like its entirety, and his relation to her has the residual deference of a patient to the doctor who knew him at his worst.

---

## 7. MERZAH THE MYSTIC — Surrogate Father / Terminal Mentor

**History.** The Himalayan monk who trains Subject in the Spurrier-era prison-of-the-mind technique. Catalogued here under family because Subject *files* him as father; the relational behaviors Subject directs at Merzah — deference, performance for approval, devastation at criticism — are paternal-bond behaviors, not student-teacher behaviors, and they map directly onto the displaced Xavier-need.

**Dynamics.** Exploitative-instructive. Merzah is, by the run's own narrative, manipulating Subject — but the manipulation works precisely because Subject is desperate to be fathered, and Merzah is willing to play the role with enough rigor to count.

**Trust/Safety.** Misplaced and ultimately betrayed; the trust was real, the object did not warrant it.

**Failure mode.** Disillusionment, which Subject metabolizes inward rather than outward.

**Leverage.** Anyone in a robe with sufficient gravitas can probably get a similar grip; the slot is structurally vacant and refills easily.

---

## 8. THE ALTERS-AS-FAMILY (Jack Wayne, Cyndi, Legion-alter, and the thousands)

**Note on inclusion.** The major alters are catalogued in full in the relevant operational sections. They are listed *here* only to register the structural fact that, for Subject, they constitute a family in the most literal sense — they are the entities he eats with, sleeps with, wakes with, ages with, and cannot leave. Jack Wayne is the older brother who bullies him. Cyndi is the younger sister who terrifies him. The Legion-alter is the uncle no one talks about. The thousands are the extended clan whose names he does not know but whose voices he cannot stop hearing at the holiday table that is his own skull.

**Dynamics.** Inescapable cohabitation. There is no door.

**Trust/Safety.** Variable per alter, generally low.

**Failure mode.** Mutiny.

**Leverage.** Whoever can address an individual alter by name acquires a relational handle the host cannot revoke.

---

## 9. THE POSTHUMOUS XAVIER-IMAGO — The Father Who Now Lives Only Inside Him

**History.** Post-AvX, Xavier exists for Subject as an internal presence reconstructed from memory, ambition, gu


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Subject's affiliative field is small, asymmetric, and weighted almost entirely toward figures who can function as parent-surrogate, confessor, or witness. He does not collect friends; he collects people who have either (a) seen the inside of his head and survived, or (b) agreed to stand near him after seeing it. The list is therefore short, the bonds heavy, and the failure modes patterned. Each entry below is constructed from the longitudinal record: first contact, evolution, observable rituals of trust, observed pattern of rupture, and the precise leverage each figure holds (and is held by).*

---

## 1. Moira MacTaggert — Custodian, First Witness, Functional Mother

**History.** Subject is delivered to Muir Island in catatonic state c. 1980s storyline-time after the embassy massacre; Moira receives him from Gabrielle Haller as a clinical responsibility and quietly adopts him as a private moral one. She is the only adult who knew him before he had a public identity, before Xavier knew he existed, and through the entirety of his catatonic years. From *New Mutants* #26–28 onward, Muir is rendered as his actual childhood home — the rooms he can navigate without looking, the kitchen where Cyndi steals sweets, the cliff path he walks when an alter is rising.

**Dynamics.** Moira is the figure he allows to medicate him, restrain him, sedate him, and design containment apparatus around his body, without resistance and without the rage-alters firing. This is staggering as a behavioral datum: a young man whose amygdala discharges psionically at minimal threat permits a woman to put devices on his skull. The implication is a pre-verbal trust laid down during the catatonia — she was the voice and the hand present when he could perceive but not respond, and that imprint holds. He calls her by first name, often softly; he does not perform for her; with her he tolerates silence, which he tolerates with no one else.

**Evolution.** From custodial (1985–early 1990s), to collaborative (he assists Excalibur-adjacent ops from Muir), to estranged-by-distance after the Legion Quest debacle (her grief over Xavier is something he cannot face), to posthumously haunting after her death and subsequent reframings in the Hickman/Krakoan reveals. Notably: even when canon retcons Moira into something other than what subject believed she was, his internal Moira — the woman who sat with him while he was a vegetable — does not update. She is fixed in him at the level the trauma lives.

**Trust / Safety.** Among the highest in the entire field. Moira is one of two figures (the other being Ruth Aldine) in whose presence subject's hair-discharge phenomena demonstrably reduce on panel. Sleep is possible near her. He has eaten food she prepared without screening it through the Karami alter for risk-assessment, which is otherwise habitual.

**Failure mode.** She cannot reach him when the grandiose-savior cognition is online; she belongs to the "patient" frame, and the grandiose frame rejects the patient frame as humiliation. Therefore at the precise moments she is most needed — the Legion Quest planning window, the Age of X construction — she is the figure he avoids most studiously. The pattern is consistent: he ghosts Moira whenever he is about to do something catastrophic. Her appearance in his perimeter is itself a behavioral marker that the system is currently *contained*; her absence is the warning sign.

**Leverage.** She holds the longest continuous file on him; she has seen every alter the original Karami inventory enumerated; she is the only person who can speak about Gabrielle to him without triggering shame. Conversely, she holds no leverage at all over the runaway states, which is why he runs from her before triggering them — to protect her from the data of her own helplessness.

---

## 2. Charles Xavier — *(cross-reference: Relationships I, FAMILY)*

Treated in the family register. Note here only the operational asset he provided briefly as mentor-figure: the X-Mansion as failed integration site, the telepathic interventions that subject experienced as the only times another mind had visited his and not shattered, and the durable internal voice that subject still negotiates with after Xavier's death. For ally-architecture purposes: Xavier is not an ally. He is the structuring absence around which all other alliances orbit.

---

## 3. The New Mutants (cohort: Mirage / Dani Moonstar, Karma / Xi'an Coy Manh, Cypher / Doug Ramsey, Magik / Illyana Rasputin, Cannonball, Sunspot, Wolfsbane / Rahne Sinclair, Warlock)

**History.** First substantive peer contact, *New Mutants* #26–28. The team enters his astral landscape to fight through his alters; this is, functionally, the first time anyone but Moira has seen the interior of him, and they did not flinch out. The experience is constitutive.

**Dynamics.** He does not bond with the team as a unit; he bonds with individual residues.
- **Dani Moonstar** — registered as the one who *named* an alter (she manifested fear-images that the Karami alter could parse); he experiences her as a peer-translator. When Dani re-enters his perimeter in later eras, his switching frequency demonstrably drops for a span of hours afterward.
- **Karma** — possession-adjacent mutant; subject has an unspoken kinship with her around the experience of being inhabited. They do not discuss it. They sit near each other and do not discuss it. This is, by his standards, intimacy.
- **Cypher** — the loss of Doug Ramsey registers in him asymmetrically heavily; he attended the death only by reputation, but Cypher's death sits in his catalog of "peers who were destroyed by being near our kind of work," which is the cognitive frame from which he later justifies isolating Ruth.
- **Magik** — fellow possessor of an internal monster; he watches her with the wariness of a man recognizing his own pathology in another body, and respects her without warmth.
- **Wolfsbane** — the only New Mutant he ever genuinely *fears*, because her religious shame-vocabulary touches the same lever in him that his Holocaust-survivor mother installed.

**Evolution.** From rescuers to colleagues to absences. He never integrates with the team as a member; he is always the patient they once helped, which is a status he cannot un-receive from them. He sends none of them postcards.

**Trust / Safety.** Moderate-functional, low-emotional. He will fight beside any of them without hesitation; he will not stay overnight in their dwellings.

**Failure mode.** They cannot stop seeing him as the boy on the table. He cannot stop being seen that way by them. The room locks both parties out of any updated relationship.

**Leverage.** Collectively, they hold the only third-party verification of his original alter inventory pre-inflation. Any one of them, invoked sincerely ("Dani would know this isn't you"), can briefly destabilize a dominant alter — the device works once per encounter and degrades with use.

---

## 4. Ruth Aldine — Blindfold — *(cross-reference: Relationships III, ROMANTIC — primary entry)*

Listed here only as chosen-family node: she is the single peer-attachment whose presence improves baseline function measurably and durably. For full architecture see the romantic register. Operational note: when Ruth is in his perimeter, the hair-phenomenon flattens, food intake regularizes, and he sleeps in measured cycles rather than collapse-and-startle. Her death/erasure removes the only somatic anchor he has ever sustained outside of monastic structure.

---

## 5. Merzah the Mystic — Late Mentor, Carceral Trainer

**History.** Himalayan monastery, *X-Men Legacy* vol. 2 #1 onward. Subject seeks him out post-Xavier's death with the explicit goal of becoming the heir to the dream his father can no longer carry. Merzah trains him to construct an internal architecture — the mental prison — in which to confine his alters.

**Dynamics.** This is the first mentor relationship subject entered as an adult, by choice, with stated goals. The dynamic is therefore qualitatively different from any prior: ritualized, ascetic, structured around demonstrable competencies (meditation hours logged, alters successfully imprisoned, breath cycles per minute). Subject thrives on the *form* — the daily order, the head-shaved discipline, the absence of women, the absence of fathers in the room, the silence. He is observed in this period to be the most physically settled he has ever been on panel: hair shorter, posture lower, voice slower.

**Evolution.** Training to graduation to grief — Merzah is killed, and the killing is, in subject's cognition, his fault by adjacency, repeating the Daniel Shomron template (older male protector → murdered while subject was present). This is the second time he loses a father-substitute violently. The internal prison Merzah taught him to build survives the teacher's death and becomes the dominant somatic-mental architecture of his late career.

**Trust / Safety.** High during training, predicated on the absence of stakes outside the monastery. The trust did not survive transposition to the outside world; once subject left the mountain, the architecture began leaking immediately.

**Failure mode.** Merzah's pedagogy treated the alters as inmates rather than parts of subject's self — a carceral, not integrative, model. This is congruent with subject's own preferred self-relation (he prefers to be a warden of himself rather than a self), and therefore felt correct, and therefore was insidious. The Merzah architecture made him *more functional* and *more disowning* simultaneously, deepening the splitting it was supposed to manage.

**Leverage.** Posthumously, Merzah's voice is one of two reliable internal counsels (the other is the Karami residue, see below). Subject can invoke Merzah's breath-instruction in moments of imminent escalation; success rate is real but degraded as the years pass without reinforcement.

---

## 6. Jemail Karami — Internal Conscience, Absorbed Other

**History.** The lead terrorist from the embassy attack, absorbed into subject's psyche at the moment of his death. Of all alters, the most morally reflective, most integrated, and least demanding of body-time.

**Dynamics.** Karami functions as the in-house ethicist. When subject's host-self is offline, Karami is the alter who will, more often than not, attempt repair behaviors — apology, restitution, calling Moira. His political and religious framework (a Palestinian, a Muslim, a man who in life chose violence and in death chose remorse) gives subject his only genuine non-Western, non-Xavierian moral vocabulary. Subject has, on occasion, used Karami's language for prayer without recognizing he is doing so.

**Trust / Safety.** Singular: this is the only entity inside subject's head he can ask a question and trust the answer of. The trust is itself diagnostic — it indicates that the most stable internal object subject possesses is the consciousness of the man who killed his stepfather. The wound and the dressing are the same tissue.

**Failure mode.** Karami is bound by the etiquette of the murderer-grateful-for-asylum. He will not seize the body to prevent catastrophe; he will only counsel. The catastrophe-prone alters know this and exploit it.

**Leverage.** Karami's residue is the lever any external party can pull to reach the host self when other alters are dominant — invoking Daniel Shomron, invoking the embassy, invoking remorse, will surface Karami, who will then mediate.

---

## 7. Rogue — Brief Peer-Custodian, Carey-era

**History.** *X-Men: Legacy* (vol. 1) under Carey. Rogue is assigned to him as effective handler at the moment of his post-headband reintegration into the X-line.

**Dynamics.** Two figures with permeable selves and damage from incorporated others recognized one another immediately. Rogue's touch-prohibition mapped neatly onto his containment-prohibition; both move through the world as people who must not let too much in. She does not flinch from the alters because she has metabolized several lives' worth of others herself.

**Evolution.** Brief, work-defined, mutually respectful. They do not become close in the chosen-family sense, but he registers her as a member of an extremely small set: "people whose psychology I do not have to translate for."

**Trust / Safety.** Operational, high. Intimate, none.

**Failure mode.** When the work ends, there is no scaffolding for the relationship to continue on. Neither party is good at the maintenance protocols of friendship.

**Leverage.** Rogue can address an alter directly and demand the host return — a maneuver requiring familiarity with his system and credibility as someone who has not been afraid of it. Among living parties post-Moira, Rogue is on the short list of those who can do this.

---

## 8. Gambit — Adjacent, Carey-era

**History.** Same period, same team configuration as Rogue.

**Dynamics.** Subject does not particularly like Gambit and does not particularly distrust him; Gambit's charm registers in subject's system as Jack Wayne–coded, which means subject is wary of him reflexively, as he is wary of his own Jack Wayne. They have a courteous, low-density working relationship in which subject mostly listens and Gambit mostly fills silence.

**Trust / Safety.** Low-medium, professional only.

**Failure mode.** None active; the relationship never carried weight enough to fail.

**Leverage.** Negligible in either direction.

---

## 9. Tyrannix the Abominoid — Companion-Imp, Spurrier-era

**History.** Acquired during the *Legacy* vol. 2 run as a kind of pocket-dimension parasite/pet that subject keeps subdued and around him.

**Dynamics.** Subject keeps a small, hostile creature alive in his vicinity that nobody else wants to keep alive — a behavior pattern that recurs (the alters themselves are also small, hostile entities he keeps alive in his vicinity). Tyrannix is the externalized form of the same drive. He talks to it the way some men talk to dogs they pretend to dislike.

**Trust / Safety.** Performative wariness, real attachment.

**Failure mode.** Tyrannix is an ablative companion — designed to suffer in his stead — and subject knows this, and the knowing is part of why he tolerates it. The relationship is structurally exploitative on his side and he is aware enough to feel low-grade guilt about it, which functions as proof to himself that he is still capable of guilt.

**Leverage.** None external; some internal, as a self-test of cruelty-thresholds.

---

## 10. Pete Wisdom and the residual British / Excalibur orbit

**History.** Adjacent to Muir; subject knows several of the British contingent peripherally from the Moira years.

**Dynamics.** Wisdom and his ilk are the only adults who treated subject in adolescence neither as patient nor as weapon nor as son-of-Xavier but as a sullen kid in a corner who could be ignored. This was a relief and is remembered as such. He retains a low, stable, unspoken fondness for the British operatives that he never explains and never acts upon.

**Trust / Safety.** Low-active, but uniquely untainted by either reverence or pity, which makes the channel clean if ever opened.

**Failure mode.** No one in this orbit will ever call him first.

**Leverage.** Available as a clean line if all other lines burn — a fact subject knows and conserves.

---

## 11. The Five Lights / Hope Summers cohort (peripheral)

**History.** Brief overlap during the post-*Second Coming* mutant resurgence; subject is adjacent to but not part of the new-mutant generation Hope gathers.

**Dynamics.** Subject experiences the younger generation as a reproach — they are what he might have been had he manifested gently — and stays at distance.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure mode.** Pre-emptive avoidance forecloses the possibility of either bond or harm.

**Leverage.** None.

---

## 12. The internal cohort, considered as chosen family

It is necessary to record what surveillance ordinarily cannot: subject's most stable cohabitants are inside his skull. **Jack Wayne**, **Cyndi**, **Karami**, the Legion-alter, and the hundreds-to-thousands of sub-alters constitute a household. He has lived with them longer than with any human. They feed and pester and protect and betray him in patterns indistinguishable from those of a chaotic extended family. The Merzah carceral model recast them as inmates, but the underlying organization is domestic: they bicker about meals, they argue over the body's clothing, they fight for the remote of attention. Surveillance protocols must therefore treat the internal cohort as an actual social network, with its own alliances and rivalries, mapped per alter where data is available (see *Capability Inventory* dossier section). The host-self's emotional center of gravity is, at most observed moments, *internal*. He is rarely alone, and the company is rarely chosen, and this is the texture of every day he has lived since age ten.

---

## 13. Aggregate observations across the ally field

- **Selection bias.** Every durable ally has either (a) seen inside his mind, (b) shared a structural feature of his pathology, or (c) been a custodial older figure with professional reason to tolerate him. There is no friend in the file who simply *liked him on meeting*.
- **Sex/gender pattern.** Mentors are male and die violently (Xavier, Merzah, by proxy Daniel Shomron). Custodial-maternal figures are female and survive him (Moira, Gabrielle, Ruth until she did not). Peer-male attachments are shallow. Peer-female attachments are deep but rare.
- **Tempo.** He does not maintain relationships across long absences without external scaffolding; the file shows almost no documented correspondence, calls, visits-without-cause. Connection requires colocation. He is, in modern terms, a man who never texts.
- **Ritual of admittance.** He admits a person to chosen-family status by allowing them to be present when an alter is surfacing without removing himself from the room. This has happened, on the record, with Moira, with Ruth, briefly with Dani, and once with Merzah. Four people across four decades.
- **Ritual of expulsion.** He expels a person from chosen-family by disappearing — geographically, dimensionally, or ontologically. He has never on record ended a relationship through conversation.
- **Aggregate leverage profile.** Any operator seeking to reach subject through his affiliative field has, in practical terms, three live channels: Moira (if living in current continuity), Ruth (if reachable), and the Karami residue (always reachable, requires correct invocation). All other channels are decorative.

— END SECTION —


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Cross-indexed to Sections 1–5 of the analytical basis. Where the subject's relational repertoire is mapped against specific named persons across the four decades of recorded behavior, the file divides naturally into three registers — those he has set himself against (rivals), those who have set themselves against him or whom he has cast in the role of antagonist (enemies), and those who have crossed the threshold of his isolation into the small zone of acknowledged intimacy (lovers/intimates). The number in each register is small. Subject does not maintain crowded rolls. Every name in this section is load-bearing.*

---

## I. RIVALS

A rival, in the operational sense this file uses, is a figure the subject measures himself against — not a person he wishes to destroy, but a person whose continued existence forces him to keep producing himself. For a subject whose entire personality architecture is a bid for paternal recognition, the rivalry slot is a structurally critical one: it holds the men he might have been, the men his father chose instead of him, and the men who occupy the heroic real estate he believes is rightfully his.

### 1. Scott Summers (Cyclops)

**History.** No direct adversarial encounter of consequence until the post-AoA period, but the relationship is structurally hostile from the moment subject becomes aware of the X-Men roster as a roster. Cyclops is the eldest acknowledged son in the family business; subject is the eldest *unacknowledged* son. Both were taken in by Xavier as boys; only one was kept. Subject's awareness of this asymmetry sharpens through the Carey *Legacy* and Spurrier *Legacy vol. 2* runs, where Cyclops occupies the moral-leadership chair subject believes he should at least be auditioned for.

**Dynamics.** Subject treats Cyclops the way a middle-aged man treats an older brother who got the inheritance: outward deference shading into uninvited critique, claims of independence pitched at exactly the volume that betrays the wish to be invited in. When Cyclops issues orders, subject obeys with the mannerism of a man being humored. When Cyclops withholds, subject performs not-needing it.

**Evolution.** Sharpens over time. In Spurrier's run the rivalry crystallizes around the *meaning of Xavier's dream*: Cyclops believes he has inherited the franchise; subject believes Cyclops *killed the man and forfeited the franchise*. This is one of the few rivalries in subject's life where the moral position is partly correct, which makes the personal venom more dangerous, not less — subject can mobilize legitimate grievance as cover for raw filial jealousy.

**Trust / safety.** Low both directions. Cyclops reads subject as an uncontained Omega liability (correct). Subject reads Cyclops as a usurper-brother (also partly correct).

**Failure mode.** Direct confrontation about the dream invariably escalates to subject's stress level 3–4 (see Section 5). Cyclops's optic blasts are tactically irrelevant against subject's defenses; the danger is reputational and relational — Cyclops can deny subject the legitimacy he covertly craves, and *that* is the ammunition.

**Leverage.** A sincere acknowledgment from Cyclops that subject is a fellow heir, not a problem to be managed, would buy more compliance than any restraint device on record. Conversely, public dismissal by Cyclops will trigger grandiose-saviorism within hours.

### 2. Merzah the Mystic

**History.** Subject's monastic tutor in the Himalayas during the bridge into *Legacy vol. 2*. Old, deliberate, untheatrical. Trains subject in the construction of the internal "mental prison" wherein the alters can be quarantined and the host can preside.

**Dynamics.** A father-substitute deliberately *chosen* — and therefore a rival to the dead father whose place he is allowed only provisionally to occupy. Subject submits to Merzah's discipline with a depth of obedience he never gave Xavier (who never asked for it). The relationship is teacherly on the surface, oedipal beneath: subject is auditioning for Merzah, and Merzah is auditioning for the role Xavier vacated.

**Evolution.** Begins as gratitude, ripens into the unbearable knowledge that what Merzah has given him is a technique, not a benediction. Subject leaves the monastery with the tools and without the love he came for. The departure scenes are characteristic: not anger, not estrangement, but a polite forward motion that reads, on review, as a son leaving a father who has failed to adopt him a second time.

**Trust / safety.** High while inside the monastery; subject is, briefly, the calmest he has ever been on record. The hair flattens noticeably in those panels — a somatic tell, and a reliable index of state.

**Failure mode.** Subject internalizes Merzah's *method* and discards Merzah's *judgment*. The prison technique, applied without the monk's continuous supervision, becomes another iteration of the lifelong pattern (Section 1: *the cure is another symptom*).

**Leverage.** Invocation of Merzah's name and ethic produces, even years later, a transient calming effect — useful in containment situations. Cannot be repeated often before subject perceives manipulation.

### 3. Magneto

**History.** The man subject travels back in time to murder (Legion Quest, 1995). One of the two most consequential people in his life he has *never actually met as adults in shared continuity of any duration*. The rivalry is entirely projective.

**Dynamics.** Magneto exists for subject not as a person but as the *obstacle between father and dream*. In the magical-grandiose cognition (Section 5: operational code), removing the obstacle should produce the love. The plan is internally coherent only inside a child's logic; subject is approximately forty years old when he executes it.

**Evolution.** After the AoA reset and subject's restoration, the rivalry becomes posthumous in a different sense: Magneto is the man subject murdered his own father trying to murder. The thought is intolerable and is, accordingly, almost never thought. When Magneto's name surfaces in subject's vicinity, the observable response is a momentary blanking — micro-dissociation, half a second, eyes unfocused — followed by topic redirection.

**Trust / safety.** Inapplicable; the relationship is mythological, not actual.

**Failure mode.** Any forced reckoning with Magneto-as-person rather than Magneto-as-symbol risks catastrophic decompensation. Subject's psyche has no protocol for the encounter.

**Leverage.** Extreme and unusable. Anyone who could orchestrate a controlled meeting between subject and Magneto, with full disclosure of the AoA history on the table, could either rebuild or destroy subject in a single conversation. No responsible actor has attempted it.

### 4. The Legion-alter (internal rival)

**History.** A self-named alter, capitalized, the most violent and grandiose of the catalogued personalities; the alter whose suppression required the unconscious construction of the Age of X pocket reality (Legacy #245–247).

**Dynamics.** The only rival who shares the body. Subject and Legion-alter are in a permanent zero-sum custody dispute over the somatic substrate. Subject's heroic project in *Legacy vol. 2* is, in plain terms, a campaign to deny Legion-alter the controls.

**Evolution.** Each cycle of restraint produces a more sophisticated Legion-alter on the other side of the restraint. The arms race is internal and unwinnable; Section 4 frames this as the over-learned successful coping strategy that can no longer be turned off.

**Trust / safety.** None possible. The alter regards the host as the obstacle in mirror image of how the host regards the alter.

**Failure mode.** Any sustained inattention by host self produces alter incursion. Sleep is the primary vulnerability.

**Leverage.** External actors who can address the alter as a separate negotiating party (rare; Karami can, Ruth eventually can) gain a channel the host cannot police.

---

## II. ENEMIES

The distinction from rivals: an enemy is a figure who has acted, or is positioned to act, to *end* the subject — not to outshine him. Subject's enemy list is shorter than one would expect for an omega-class mutant with a planet-scale rap sheet, because most of the people who would qualify are dead, fictional within his own mind, or himself.

### 1. The Shadow King (Amahl Farouk)

**History.** Encountered on the Muir Island astral planes during the late Claremont period and resurgent across subsequent X-line crossovers. A predatory telepath who feeds on division, fear, and dissociated psychic material — i.e., a being for whom subject's interior is an open buffet.

**Dynamics.** The Shadow King relates to subject the way a parasite relates to a host with a compromised immune system. He does not need to defeat subject; he needs only access. Every alter is a potential doorway; every unintegrated trauma is an unlocked window.

**Evolution.** The pattern across encounters is consistent: Shadow King probes, subject's defenses fragment further, an alter is suborned or amplified, containment is achieved at high cost by external actors (Moira, Xavier in life, the wider X-roster). Subject is rarely the agent of his own deliverance in these contests, which compounds the shame load.

**Trust / safety.** Zero. The Shadow King is the one entity whose threat model maps perfectly onto subject's vulnerability surface.

**Failure mode.** Direct telepathic engagement on Shadow King's terms is an automatic loss condition. Subject knows this and knows that he *forgets* it under stress.

**Leverage.** None available to subject. The Shadow King holds essentially all of it: knowledge of the alter directory, capacity to whisper to specific alters past the host's knowledge, willingness to wait years between strikes.

### 2. The Age of Apocalypse counterfactual

**History.** Not a person; a *world* subject created and unmade. Listed here because it functions, in subject's interior life, as a personified accuser.

**Dynamics.** Every death in the AoA continuity is on the ledger subject carries. The dead are not abstract — Xavier's death scene in his arms is replayed in flashback across multiple writers' hands, and subject demonstrably re-experiences it (Section 4: intrusive imagery, C-PTSD domain).

**Evolution.** The accusation does not weaken with time. It is the single most reliable trigger for Section 5's escalation cascade.

**Trust / safety.** Inapplicable; the enemy is internal-historical.

**Failure mode.** Any narrative reactivation of the AoA history (cyclical in X-line publishing) places subject at elevated decompensation risk for the duration of the arc and several months past it.

**Leverage.** Held by anyone — friend or foe — willing to invoke the AoA. Foes do so to disable; friends do so, sparingly, to ground subject in a guilt that, paradoxically, has been one of his more stabilizing affective anchors. Guilt he can hold; love he cannot.

### 3. Charles Xavier (post-mortem)

**History.** Father, withheld then revealed, killed by the subject's own hand in a timeline reset, then restored, then killed again by another (Cyclops, AvX). Listed in the enemies register *because the dead father is the most active and unappeasable adversary in subject's life*. He occupies enemy slot more than rival slot at this stage: he can no longer be won, only fled or appeased through gesture.

**Dynamics.** Subject conducts a continuous one-sided dialogue with the absent Xavier — sometimes audibly, often subvocally, occasionally through alter-mediated channeling that mimics the father's voice with eerie fidelity. Behaviorally indistinguishable, at moments, from a haunting.

**Evolution.** Xavier-as-living-father was conditional, distant, but theoretically reachable. Xavier-as-dead-father is unconditional in his withholding — the silence cannot be revised. Subject's worsening in the Spurrier arc tracks directly to this finalization.

**Trust / safety.** The relationship has passed the threshold beyond which trust is a coherent variable.

**Failure mode.** Any encounter with Xavier-iconography (the school, the chair, the X, even certain phrasings) produces measurable physiological response: pupil dilation, postural fixation, hair-line crackle.

**Leverage.** Anyone who can credibly *speak for* Xavier — claim posthumous instruction, channel the voice, produce an unread letter — holds a lever subject cannot resist examining. This is the largest single vulnerability in the file.

### 4. Mister Sinister (peripheral)

**History.** Recurring threat across the *Legacy* and Krakoan periods; a geneticist for whom an omega-class mutant with a fractured psyche is a uniquely interesting acquisition target.

**Dynamics.** Sinister relates to subject as biomaterial. Subject's response is uncharacteristically clean — *something in him recognizes Sinister as not-father, not-rival, not-mirror, and the absence of relational hook produces, briefly, a clarity he rarely otherwise demonstrates*. Useful diagnostic datum: when subject is not psychologically implicated, his judgment is unremarkable to good.

**Trust / safety.** None offered, none expected. The cleanest enemy slot in the file.

**Failure mode.** Subject underestimates the practical-tactical dimension because the relational dimension is null.

**Leverage.** Limited both directions.

### 5. Self

**History.** Lifelong. The enemy of longest standing.

**Dynamics.** Twice, on the record, subject has resolved the antagonism by removing himself from existence (Legion Quest reset, Spurrier finale). The intervening years are a managed truce.

**Failure mode.** When all other containment fails, this is the alter-of-last-resort: the host-as-executioner. It is not depression in the standard sense; it is the conviction, calmly held, that subject's non-existence is a public good. Section 4 logs this under MDD-with-suicidality; the *quality* of the ideation is not despair but accounting.

**Leverage.** Held by anyone who can convince subject that his continued existence does *measurable good to a specific named person*. Abstract worth-arguments fail. Particular ones (Ruth, on the record; in principle, a child, were one ever to exist) work.

---

## III. LOVERS AND INTIMATES
